SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 28, 1891.
“A Vote for Thrift.”
A correspondent of the Sydney Daily
Telegraph, subscribing himself Colin M.
Smith, writes in a spirit of weird exaltation
regarding the voting deservingness of an
alleged virtue yclept “ Thrift.” " His letter
is, on the whole, praiseworthily temperate,
and his English is a peg or so Higher in
calibre than the average importer s mer-
cantile epistolary jargon ; but his logic is
pitifully erratic, and his arguments would be
considered eccentric and wildly original even
in the fabled land of paradox that Dean
Swift lias immortalised under the designa-
tion of Laputa. In the first place, he con-
founds a moral quality (if it is a moral
quality), with a principle of political economy
springing from an arbitrarily-adopted sys-
tem ; and, in the second place, he demands
a reward for his specified virtue out of all
proportion to its assumed value, and to the
exclusion of all other moral qualities what-
soever.
# * #
Thrift is not Capital. So manifestly is
this the case that the cultivation of “ thrift ”
is usually in exactly inverse ratio to the
amount of capital possessed by the culti-
vator. The father of nine girls—not one of
whom he can marry off his hands—who is in
receipt of a salary which meets about two-
thirds of the just and Necessary require-
ments of his family, is ordinarily the man
we expect to hud manufacturing a virtue out
of necessity—in which case necessity takes
the guise of virtue and calls itself “Thrift.”
To argue the possession of as much “ Thrift ”
by an individual as pounds sterling, or bank-
shares, or mining-scrip or debentures, or
stock of any character whatsoever that he
may likewise possess, is ridiculous. Capital
is always the same fluctuating, non-moral,
non-omotional, non-intellectual integer, no
matter who possesses it ; and one might as
well attempt to assess the mental faculties of a
horse by the number of nails in his shoes as
the moral qualities of a man (Thrift in-
cluded) by the number of guineas he
possesses. Take an example ; One thousand
navvies are necessary to perform a certain
amount of work, and a capital of ten
thousand pounds is necessary to put this
labour into motion. Now this sum may be
the savings of ten-thousand widows (who,
being women, would have no vote at all), or
it may be a paltry speculatory driblet from
the coffers of a Gould. In all human pro-
bability where should we look for the
“Thrift” here? —in the case of the
millionaire, or in the case of the widows ?
* * *
“Thrift” is a moral quality. Capital is
not a moral quality ; and things unlike can-
not be compared. Nay, more, the terms
Capital and Labour, used in a comparative
sense, are likewise absurd. Labour is a term
representing human endeavour and neces-
sarily human interest. The thing called
Capital—(no matter how it is owned or
directed) —is only the tool by which Labour
—(no matter whose) —is enabled to operate.
The one thousand navvies represent a bundle
of human aims and aspirations which cannot
morally be abridged or curtailed by the
ten thousand pounds necessary to put their
labour into motion. The ten thousand pounds
may be owned by ten thousand widows ; or
five thousand, or five hundred, or twenty, or
ten shareholders—or one ; it carries with it
no corresponding weight of human interest.
It is mere operating-power, always fluctuat-
ing in value, always changing hands, and
never stopping in one pair of hands long
enough for one to count it.
» * «
Colin M. Smith simply claims a vote for
Capital, which ho calls by the name of
“Thrift”—although it is manifestly ridi-
culous to regard the accumulators of Capital
as the most thrifty amongst men, or to regard
“ Thrift ” itself as a virtue so exalted as to
demand a reward above all other virtues—
and, to be strictly logical, he would doubt-
less be agreeable to measure out voting power
according to the amount of capital {i e.,
“ Thrift ') possessed at the time the electoral
rolls were made up. In other words, a fall
in the price of stocks would so depreciate
the value of Brown’s property—(that is, his
moral quality, called “Thrift”) —that he
would be disfranchised until a lucky specu-
lation enabled him once more to pose as a
virtuous man and a duly qualified elector.
* # *
Mr. Smith is so fully convinced of the
righteousness of politically - empowered
wealth, that he makes the following mani-
festly incorrect and misleading statement :
Under some rule of “ one shareholder one vote ” most of
the splendid achievements of our time would have been
impossible.
Now this sounds plausible ; but it is not true.
Many of these achievements we are accus-
tomed to designate as “ splendid ” are wind-
blown frauds, fraught with subsequent
disaster to millions ; many of these achieve-
ments have been national undertakings con-
spicuously adopted by those countries the
most democratic in their mode of Govern-
ment—no one hears of “splendid achieve-
ments ” being realised in such countries as
Russia, for instance. Of course, we exclude
from the category of “splendid achieve-
ments ” all wars, whether undertaken for
the “advancement of commerce” or other-
wise ; and one can readily appreciate the
eagerness of a capitalistic and commercial
voting-power to dominate and direct the
fighting force of a nation —the recollection of
England’s last disgraceful Egyptian war is
yet fresh in our minds.
# * *
This, however, is, a digression. To
return to the subject: many of the
most conspicuously “splendid achieve-
ments ” of our age have originated in the
brains of poor men who, not having capital
to develope their ideas, have been com-
pelled to sell their interests to a robber
band of speculators, and the exceptional and
non-original means thus put in the hands of
these gentlemen enabled them to amass
wealth, morality, and voting power at the
same instant, thus effectually gaining a
monopoly of all the virtue extant on the
earth. In some few cases “splendid achieve-
ments ” have been perfected to their full
fruitage by men possessing little monetary
or political power, by the intensity of their
convictions, by the reasonableness and the
desirability of their ideals, and by the
magnetism of their personality. We do say,
however, and say it witli a strong sense of
certitude, that not in this, or in any other
age whatsoever since man has achieved con-
sciousness, has the possession of mere
operating power (call it Capital, or “Thrift,”
or exploitation, or any other name within
the two covers, of a dictionary) advanced by
one single fraction of an iota anything within
the memory of man that he has the audacity
to call “ splendid,” or the courage to desig-
nate by the ambitious title of an “achieve-
ment.”
* # #
But, apart from this question as to the
identity of “Thrift” with the possession of
Capital, there arises one other question—
namely, is “Thrift,” taking it in its ordi-
dinary acceptation, so superlative a virtue ?
What is it, mainly 1 The ability to take
care of number-one ; and number-one, having
taken care of himself up to a certain stage,
calmly turns round and demands from
number-two, &c., certain privileges which
shall make it impossible for anybody else
to take care of himself for all eternity.
“Thrift,” once said Blake, the poet, “is a
disgusting and penurious old prude courted
by Incapacity. ’ Of course, Mr. Smith does
not mean that sort of “ Thrift.” The virtue
he has in his eye is not the careful stretch-
ing of a yard of elastic across a three-chain
road without snapping it. Mr. Smith’s
thrifty man is the clever financier who can
angle for millions with a bait constructed
out of an adroit prospectus and the masterful
manipulation of probabilities. Wherever
“Thrift” per se made a rich man out of a
poor one, the hand of God was in evidence
and a miracle was being worked. The
“Thrift ’ most people know of is going
without that which you ought to have but
which you cannot get, and nobody is thrifty
in this sense when circumstances favour the
getting of it, unless he be a fool or a rogue,
or be else so low in the social scale as to
deserve the name neither of fool or rogue.
The man useful to humanity does not pare
cheese and bait his rat-trap on the smell of
the rind which he has eaten himself. No
man lives who has made a fortune by
“Thrift,” and if he did live, the mere fact
of his existence brands him so effectually as
a creature not fit to exist, from his enmity to
his kind, that while capital punishment is
permitted to exist in any shape he should
be immediately taken forth and hanged
from the nearest lamp-post,
* * #
We do not, therefore, regard “Thrift”
as one virtue among ten thousand. If
we did so regard it, we could hardly
consent to reward it virtue is, perhaps
unfortunately, its own reward ; at all
events, we never heard of any of the
moral qualities attaining the dignity of par-
liamentary representation, although most
legislative associations have the reputation
of being the home and family cradle of
nearly all the vices in the calendar. The
ability to take care of oneself may be a very
“ splendid achievement,” but it is usually so
deeply implanted in most men that it needs
very little artificial encouragement: especi-
ally as, at present, those who cannot, or will
not, take care of themselves are promptly
sent to the lunatic asylum. If we arc to
reward the man who makes his own position
secure, what are we to do for the man whose
concern in life is the welfare of others 1 If
“Thrift” is to be rewarded, what of Benevol-
ence ? The ancient Greeks had other ideas
than we in regard to this matter. To be
rich while in office was so much of a crime
that men were frequently arraigned and
expatriated on no more serious ground.
A r Ist ms gave away all his property when
appointed to a position in the public service,
lest he should be accused of what Mr.
Smith calls “Thrift.” Lest Parkes, upon
reading this, should immediately pose as a
modern Aristides, we hasten to inform him
that the ancient political Greek was a firm
believer in the ideal of Fenelon, that a man
who served the State should die leaving
neither property nor debts. Pauperism in
a politician was in ancient Greece a virtue,
for the Greeks argued—and rightly—that a
man could not possibly serve equally
honestly both himself and the State. Again,
if we reward any one moral quality at all,
why should we not reward the whole of the
moral qualities ?
* # #
We are playing with words ! It comes
simply to this, Mr. Colin M. Smith does
not demand a vote for “ Thrift.” In reality,
he asks a vote for Capital as opposed to
Human Interests of all and every kind. He
says, in eftect, “strengthen the position of
those who already have in such a manner as
to render them impregnable, and to put
beyond the pale of possibility the employ-
ment of the same means by which they
attained that position by others who may
desire to do likewise.” In the commercial
world first is ever victor. The political
entrenchment of Capital would mean the
permanent disabling of would-be Capital,
“ Thrift’’-promoted or otherwise. But,
and here comes into consideration a fric-
tional interest--a vote for “Thrift,”
that is ability to operate financially,
would not favour real Capital-holders to
half the extent that Mr. Colin M. Smith
imagines. The men who would reap the
advantage are those risky geniuses who can
skate on the edge of a financial ice-floe with-
out coming to much harm, and who exploit
hand-craft and brain-sweat with one hand
while they tear the vitals out pf the real,
solid and genuine capitalist on the other—
ex. grat., a facile-brained adventurer like
Mortimer H. Franklyn preying on the
vitals of the representatives of wealthy
integrity like the Hektys— and their in-
fluence in a State, instead of building up its
prosperity, tends ever to disaster and
ruin. The empire of Lons Napoleon was
such a financial bubble, and the French
Imperial Government fell with a crash as
soon as its credit was assailed. It was not
ended by its conquest by Germany, for it
had already ceased to exist as soon as it was
found necessary to go to war in order to
insure financial stability by a victory.
Carthage was a State in which all power
was vested in the hands of a commercial
oligarchy. The moment Carthage pushed
her devotion to money-making beyond a
point necessary to national salvation, and
intrusted her defence to paid mercenaries,
she fell. The life of a commercial State is
simply the life of any business undertaking
the nation must pay so much per cent, on
the capital invested, or collapse. Venice
and Genoa began in the deification of the
three golden balls ; and now they are whole-
sale water-side bagnios for courtezans and
princes-—“ the reward of Thrift.”
The Great Land Swindle.
Australia is pre-eminently the home of land-
speculation and land-swindles. There is pro-
bably more land in the market in Australia, in
proportion to the population, than there is in
any other country on earth. The principal
crop of this fertile continent is a harvest of arid
boards bearing the inscription: “These allot-
ments for sale,” and a hundred thousand fields
which ought to be rich with fruit or grain grow
nothing but a glaring placard apiece, announcing
that a desirable property is to be offered by
public auction. Nearly half the land-holders
of Australia never dream of turning the soil to
any profitable purpose ; they have never learned
to regard it as a means for the sustenance of
humanity, but only as a substance to be carved
out in small sub-divisions, and traded off on
instalment principles. There are more estate-
agents to the square mile in Australia than in
any other country known to geography ; also,
there are more building societies, land com-
panies, auctioneers, and syndicates in these
provinces than in any other civilised region.
There are six times as many allotments for sale
as a population of three million souls can
reasonably purchase, and any individual who
seems to want a patch of ground is liable to be
trodden to death by the frantic rush of agents
all desirous of making over to him all sorts and
varieties of property. Several thousands of
men scattered over every part of this continent
have no visible means of existence except by
loafing in a dingy office in company with a
dog-cared ledger, and waiting for a chance to
dispose of a quarter-acre of ground to any
victim who chances to pass by. Some of them
are willing to sell for a small deposit down, and
the balance to be paid in the dim, indefinite
future ; some require no deposit at all, and pro-
fess not to want the balance till the Day of
Judgment ; some want nothing whatever, by
their own showing, and will lend the buyer
money to build with as well. Most of them
simply put down in their books any property
that chances to be for sale, and wrest a com-
mission out of the vendor if they happen to
find a customer, and, as a consequence, twelve
or fifteen members of the tribe often live, or
attempt to live, on the effort to sell one allot-
ment which, as likely as not, proves unsaleable.
Also, they all have money to lend in sums
varying from £5O up to £5O 00Q, at the shortest
notice.
* # #
The average property-agent is a mystery.
As a rule, he is a dusty, seedy, middle-aged
individual, with a shifty eye, and a brown coat,
and a dusty, hard hat, and a wooden expression
like the side of a hut. Very few people ever
saw an old property-agent, or a young one,
and nobody ever attended a dead agent’s
funeral. ,1:1 is office is generally up a stair,
in a dark building opening out of a lane.
It consists mostly of one room, with an
ink-splashed deal table in it, and two inky
chairs, and a murky window, and there are
huge blots on the floor, as if he had wept ink
in some moment of depression when trade was
unusually bad. £ ery few people ever saw an
agent sell any property in one of these dens, or
found him collecting rent from anybody, or
discovered him in the act of lending a large
sum to anybody on mortgage ; and not one man
in a hundred, on going into the office, ever met
another man coming out. The inhabitant of
the premises is always found writing a letter
of some kind to an alleged client, and pre-
sumably he posts the letter in tire inside pocket
of his old, brown coat, and leaves it there to
all futurity. He comes early and keeps open
kite, with a dreary, yellow gas-jet flaring over
his head ; he never seems to think about any-
thing in particular ; he cats sadly at a second-
rate restaurant, and he is too sad to get drunk
—a jovially-intoxicated property-agent is an
unknown quantity. Presumably he lives some-
where, but this is not certain. He is only a
solemn, brown anomaly in an office, and appar-
ently he exists by sitting hard down on a chair,
and Ins use to the world at large is not worth
mentioning.
* * *
Now and then, however, some abnormally
bright and energetic specimen of the brother-
hood sells a suburban allotment on time-pay-
ment, generally to a member of the working
classes—the orthodox, dismal, faded agent never
sells anything. He probably collects a small
deposit, and executes a document by wdiich
his alleged principal owns up to the transaction,
and agrees to transfer the property in due legal
form, on receipt of the last instalment at some
period probably 10 years ahead. The buyer
generally takes the existence of this principal
for granted ; he also takes the ownership of the
land for granted ; and assumes that the title is
correct and thoroughly reliable. He further
assumes that there is no mortgage on the pro-
perty, or, if there is one, that the owner, or
the alleged owner, will pay the interest regu-
larly and meet the principal in due time. Or,
if he is sufficiently business-like to investigate
these points, and finds that everything is in
good order, he still has to assume that the
landholder will not mortgage the property at
some future time, or, if he does, that he will
meet the liability honourably ; that he
will not sell the land to a dozen different
individuals and mortgage it as well, and bolt
with the profits of the- nefarious transaction ;
that he will not go bankrupt f and that the
dreary middleman with the dismal countenance,


1 (he dismal office, and the hard hat, will
, ~„nblv pay over the money to his principal
lol f there is one—instead of embezzling it
1 vanishing. Lastly, he takes it as read that
a i coll pr will convey the land when the payments
th ® colll pleted, instead of simply taking all his
aF 1 and then laughing at him—as he is
m ° hied to do by the fact that the preliminary
f n , ovnenses involved in compelling the vendor
S £ article that I,as been bought and
■1 for are beyond the reach of the ordi-
palv humble investor. Among all these
h nces the position of the individual who
ft' U p a time-payment allotment is about as
Serous as that of a heretic in the Middle
an experimentalist who hauls on the
til of a tiger. # #
In New South Wales, at all events, the per-
on who sells land is one of the few who is
allowed to acquire money by false pretences
Sth absolute impunity. The dull, dusty
henomenon in the office is himself an almost
sufficient evidence of the dishonesty of his call-
j- or i ie is much too numerous to be supported
Kv the humble per centage which is supposed to
fall to his lot. The land-jobber may mortgage
1 is land to one individual and at the same time
sell it on- the instalment plan to another, but
though he takes all the latter’s savings he is in
uo way compelled to use the money in clearing
off the mortgage. He may simply appropriate
both the mortgage money and the purchase
money, and then go insolvent, in which case
the mortgagee takes everything, and the buyer
loses all his little pile, and has no redress. The
seller, of course, knows that the unpaid mort-
gagee’must foreclose ;he knows also that the
weak and confiding speculators who are toiling
to pay up the purchase money of their quarter-
acre allotments will never get anything
in return; and as he takes their coin through
a long series of years under the pro-
mise that they will receive a legal title
in exchange, which promise he deliberately
makes of no account, he thereby obtains money
by false pretences in the lowest, meanest, and
most degrading sense of the term. He lives by
absorbing the earnings of the industrious and
provident classes all through the best years of
their lives, and then reduces them to ruin in
their old age ; he softly absorbs the savings of
the household bread-winner, and when that
victim has lived and died in the endeavour to
make some provision for his family he leaves
the widow and the orphans to be thrown out
by the mortgagee. He is many degrees baser
and more heartless than the three-card man or
the totalisator fiend, and he compares badly
with the ordinary garrotter. But the laws are
mostly made by this particular variety of
vampire, and therefore his miserable fraud is
strictly protected by statute. If he were
allotted seven years penal servitude like any
other common swindler, more than one
prominent politician whom the world will here
name would now be pounding road-metal under
Government supervision.
# * *
The remedy for this astonishing state of
things is sufficiently simple. Every individual
selling land on the instalment plan should be
required to deposit the deeds in the Titles
Office, and to furnish the buyers with a certifi-
cate from that office stating that they have been
so deposited, and that they are free from all
encumbrance. There would be no hardship in
this, for —the land once sold—the seller could
not possibly want the deeds, save for purposes
of fraud, and he could make no possible proht
out of them by any honest process. They
cease, in fact, to belong to him from the
moment the sale is effected, and no individual
can claim to trade off a property and still keep
it in his own hands. Any attempt to sell land
on the instalment plan while a mortgage remains
upon it, or to sell it without depositing the
deeds, should be treated as a felony. If the
vendor requires immediate accommodation he can
mortgage the unpaid instalments to any extent
he pleases; in other words, he will be at liberty to
hypothecate his own interest, but will no longer
be permitted to trade off interests that are
not his own. The man who sells land would
then be placed on the same footing as the person
who sells any other material substance ; he
would be required to deliver the article for
which he had received payment, and any
attempt to obtain payment on a false pretence
of making delivery would be treated in the
same way as are false pretences in general. It
is a small thing to ask that a landlord should
not be allowed to sell the same article to two
different buyers, and take the value ■ from
each of them. But, so far, the legal
element in the Legislature has shown itself
strongly opposed to any change. The barrister,
as another variety of common swindler who is
authorised to take retainers under the false
pretence of doing work which he frequently
leaves undone, naturally sympathises with his
brother cormorant, and so far the people have
taken no effectual steps to gaol either of them,
but of late the land-instalment iniquity has
become too great a burden. The old, mysterious
property agent in his murky, ink-blotted office
was an incubus, but at the worst he was a
retail incubus, and he did his frauds in a small
and humble fashion ; but the insolvent “banks ”
and building societies, and the big land-boom
bends of recent years—which are the same
murky individual on a larger and hungrier
scale-have intensified the evil, and the case is
becoming every day more urgent.
Guaranteeing the Gallows.
A Sydney daily with approving unction quotes
irom Frisco Argonaut the subjoined paragraph
asi being unpleasant reading for the “ cranks”
who would abolish capital punishment:—
abolition of capital punishment is being written

antcrmiKht be punished with hanging, had this expres-
: .' They tops a cove out here for slugging a bloke ”
, ean mg they hang a convict out here for assaulting a
aider). “That bit of a rope, dear Jack, is a great check
° n a man’s temper.”
Hie would-bo abolitionists of capital punish-
ment, among whpm The Bulletin is proud to
ei) tol itself, might have sought far ere finding
such an admirable argument to sustain their
own contentions as is here placed in their hands
by tire very greasers of the gallows-rope them-
selves. A convict’s testimony is urged in proof
of the assumption that capital punishment is
deterrent. .Now, a punishment is deterrent
in proportion to the certainty of its infliction,
in an inverse ratio to the probabilities
of escape. In the case of an imprisoned
convict, the certainty of detection of, and
of execution for, murder is practically abso-
lute ; the possibility, to say nothing of any
probability, of escape, nil. A prisoner-mur-
derer must slay his victim with the “bit of a
rope ” actually dangling in bis sight, with his
feet virtually on, and voluntarily placed upon,
the gallows-trap. Yet, are there not numerous
instances on record of prisoners having mur-
dered their fellows—either co-prisoners, or
(more frequently) prison officials? If the in-
violable practice of “ topping coves ” absolutely
put an end to all murders (or even murderous
assaults) by prisoners—an achievement the due
fulfilment of which could only be certified to by
the man who outlived the existence of all
prisons—there might be some reason in the
arguments of the hangmen; but even then,
only so far as would-be prisoner-murderers are
concerned. The same walled-in certainties of
detection and strangulation can never be applied
to incubating murderers who enjoy personal
liberty. To the free man, the gallows is only a
distant and a contingent menace and very often
not even so much as that. A hundred possible
loopholes of escape present themselves to his
mind. He may avoid detection, as untold
numbers do. How many secret poisoners have
never been found out? How many actual
murderers live still, unsuspected, honoured,
and even venerated ? It is at least analogically
probable that the percentage of uncaught
murderers is as large as that of any other class
of undetected offenders, and the records of
police bafflement place the latter at a high
average. Who can estimate how many success-
ful murderers stalk at large in Australia alone ?
Or the prospective murderer may forecast
equal chances of another being wrongfully
hanged or otherwise killed in his stead—as has
frequently happened. Who can say how many
Australian murders alone have been wrongfully
debited ? Camp-fire stories of dark deeds in
the solitude of the bush are overloaded with
such probable incidents —the isolation of two
men ; the swift bullet-messenger of one ; then
the falsely-witnessing aboriginal spear thrust
into the otherwise tell-tale aperture and left
there ; the finding of the body and the hurried
verdict against, and subsequent “ dispersion ”
of, the nearest blacks; all this is but a
commonplace phase of many possible variations.
* * *
And always present to the mind of a sane in-
tending murderer is one open avenue of escape
that looms in his meditative horizon as a certain
alternative to the gallows —that is suicide. He
argues that he can always take his own life.
When the avengers are on his track, it often
happens that he doesn’t do so—for varying
reasons; sometimes, but not often, out of newly-
born fear; at others, on the offchance of possible
acquittal by a jury. But while he is in the
stage preparatory to murder, the only stage to
which the gallows can act as a warning, the
suicide exit is with him a strong argument in
favor of his chances of escaping the rope.
Therefore, if hanging were held to be a de-
terrent to would-be prisoner-murderers—which
the facts of prison-life absolutely disprove—it
does not operate on free men or women outside.
What possible class of murderers does it deter ?
The murderer who, in an overwhelming fury of
hate, vows that he will have an enemy’s life,
and “ damn the consequences ? ” If one is in
that frame of mind and acts rrp to it, he or she
—frenziedly or coolly, according to tempera-
ment —kills the victim, gallows or no gallows.
He or she may be sorry afterwards, but that is
not what the gallows-man claims to want. He
claims to want him or her to be afraid
beforehand. Another sort of murderer kills
for gain. Hoes the gallows deter him ?
Never. All his ingenuity is expended in setting
his toils cunningly, in doing the deed secretly
and leaving no sign—in short, in fooling the
gallows. He fancies himself very much more
clever than all the murderers who have pre-
ceded him —it is the same with all your clever
embezzlers—and he is deterred, not from com-
mitting murder, but (as he thinks) only to the
extent of being found out. Occasionally he is
found out and hanged ; but that doesn’t prevent
others from risking the chance of detection, any
more than the frequency of shipwrecks deters
sailors from going to sea, or the accidents to
performing parachutists deter others from fling-
ing themselves from balloon-cars, for gain.
Until it can be made an every-day fact that the
detection of murder is inevitable, and a mur-
derer’s escape from the gallows is impossible,
the argument that hanging (or any other form
of capital punishment) is a deterrent leaks very
badly. Another variety of murderer is the one
who, so far as human enquiry can fathom, is
not swayed by any accountable motive ; he is
thick-skulled and brutish, and kills his
victim as horses or oxen might crush
obstacles beneath their hoofs. No argu-
ment could prevail against a murderer of
this class ; therefore, no displayed threats of
punishment could act upon him as deterrents.
# * *
The otherwise murderer, who is impression-
able enough to be scared from his purpose by a
dioramic vision of the gallows, is impossible ;
such an imagined being never could be a
murderer, for something or other would occur
to water his purpose. When the real impulse
to murder is aroused in the human breast, it is
an irresistible passion, overmastering and all-
effacing, except that it may have the chance
adjunct of cunning that attempts to defeat con-
sequences. It may also co-exist—and in the
prison instances probably always does co-exist
—with that absolute nausea of, life which
prompts suicide. Now, if men can’t resist the
impulse of suicide—that revolt against self-
preservation, the first law of nature—what fear
of punishment shall operate as a deterrent of
murder? Tiro prevalence of suicide furnishes a
strong argument against the gallows-pleaders.
The fact that some murderers commit suicide to
escape the gallows does not effect the efficacy of
this argument at all, since they did not refrain
from becoming murderers, and, in fact, the off-
chance of suicide was one of the assistant in-
centives which enabled them to proceed with
their desperate crimes. So much in absolute
disproof of the contentions of the gallowsphiles.
In the more pertinent enquiry as to the
iniquity of capital punishment, it is sufficient
to add mention of its chief and utterly inde-
fensible stain—the very nature of its operation
prevents it from ever rectifying a mistake.
When it does make a mistake, which is fre-
quently, it commits the very crime which it is
supposed to punish and prevent. When the
law kills the wrong person it makes its agents
murderers. And those agents form the only
class of murderers who always escape the
gallows.
* # *
As we wrote the foregoing last week,
two convicts lay in New South Wales gaols
awaiting the rope —(1) Maurice Dalton, a
grey-headed murderer who had by four years
exceeded man’s allotted life-span, and (2)
Harold Mallalieu, a criminal exactly half-a-
century younger. Dalton, a drunkard,
appears to have slain his nagging Avife in a fit
of ungovernable rage—temporary insanity—
arising out of her reproaches at his unwilling-
ness or inability to earn his living (though, as a
matter of fact, there is no absolute evidence that
his was the hand which killed her); Mallalieu
took the life of his mate, as the latter slept, in
the lonely bush, far remote from any human eye
save that of the assassin, who did not expect to be
found out, having made arrangements accord-
ingly. Where is the deterrent to the respective
classes of criminals or lunatics to which these
condamnes belong? And as in Mallalieu’s
case, so was it in the case of Hansen, executed
the other day for killing his mate, Duncker,
on the Broken-hill road. Hansen carefully
arranged that he should never be found out,
but he was detected after all. Yet no man
ever learns in such matters from any other
man’s experience—
All men think all men mortal but themselves ;
Themselves, when some alarming; shock of fate
Strikes through the wounded heart the sudden dread.
Mrs. Maybrick was convicted of poisoning her
husband, but she had arranged for not being
found out; and Mrs. Louisa Collins, of Sydney,
who had also arranged for escaping, poisoned
one and probably two husbands, and was found
out after all. And so all along the grim list of
the world’s murderers. Deliberate cold-blooded
murder (as in the Avell-known Sydney case of
Nicholls and Lester) is regarded by many —
more than people know of—as only an adven-
turous way of getting a living, like file-grinding
or shooting Niagara.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Queensland Immigration Vote.
The Queensland Coalition Cabinet has just got
its estimates through, including an item of
£64,000 for immigration purposes. Queens-
land, at the present moment, is in a woefully
bad way —in a worse way, probably, than any
other of the Australian provinces. There is
more hunger, more hopeless, abject misery, in
Brisbane alone than in any other Australian
city of twice the size, and the Premier’s office
is haunted by hunger-stricken deputations of
unemployed demanding work of any kind at
almost any price. The dull and callous
Griffith has one stereotyped answer to these
appeals, and it consists of a general assur-
ance that “no one shall be allowed to
starve in Queensland.” His petitioners are
starving ; their very presence gives the
lie direct to the smug politician who
insults their misery ; and they are left
to go on starving just as before. The blatant
Hodgkinson is ltill less sympathetic; he
merely announces in the Assembly that there
are “ a hundred places” in Queensland where
any man can get employment if he cares to go
there and look for it, but as HodgkinsoK never
gives the address of even one of those places he
is either coarsely indifferent to the sufferings of
the men and women and hungry children of
Queensland —or else he is lying. As for
Treasurer MTlwkaith, he only smiles his old,
fat, whiskyfied smile, and skirmishes after
fresh loans. Meanwhile, £64,000 are to be
extracted from the distressed population in order
to pay for bringing in more population to increase
the distress, and when the new consignments of
cheap paupers arrive, and can find no employ-
ment, and are run in for vagrancy, and sent to
gaol for sleeping on doorsteps, then they will
be taxed in their turn to bring in more cheap
paupers, and so on ad infinitum. The Queens-
land Government maintains an immigration
lecturer whose sole business it is to tell astound-
ing legends about Queensland as a new Paradise,
and the immigrants who have been attracted
by his soulful voice and his garbled statistics
are sleeping in boilers on the wharves at all the
ports from the Brisbane River up to the Gulf.
The province is choke-full of impoverished
humanity ; to bring in more under present con-
ditions is as utterly brutal as to import fresh
immigrants into a beleaguered city that is
already in the last stage of famine. If the
immigration money were dropped into a
quicksand or sent to the Imperial Insti-
tute it would be better expended ; in that in-
stance, it would only be lost—in the present
case it is employed to create untold suffering,
and expended in the purchase of misery. But
the Coalition, apparently, has no desire in
heaven or earth except to get cheap labour to
cultivate big estates ; its one ambition seems to
be to bring down wages, and thereby make
money for the monopolists, and the one sure
way to bring down wages is by creating hunger
and utter wretchedness. The four gods of the
Queensland Ministry are beef and hides and
tallow and wool, and it recognises nothing
beyond them. It is an utterly soulless and
animal aggregation of fat and grovel and cash,
and it would be better for its good name in the
days to come that its immigration vote had
been turned into coppers and hung round its
neck, and then that it had been dropped into
the Pacific and forgotten.
Those -whom the Lord hath Joined.
Last week Judge Windeyeb, sitting in state
and horse-hair wig for the purpose of putting
.asunder those whom the Lord had joined in
New South Wales, was induced to make some
vigorous comments on the stony brutality of the
divorce-law of that province. The case under
discussion was that of Barbara Freeman, who
testified that she had been kicked for thirty-
one years, in pursuance of the holy sacrament
of marriage. According to her evidence—which
Mr. Freeman seemingly made no effort to dis-
prove —she had, while in delicate health, been
flogged by the individual who took her for
better or worse, and thrown down a well thirty-
five feet deep, with ten feet of water at the
bottom. Then Mr. Freeman hauled her up
and dropped her down again, and hauled her
up again. She had been jumped on with
Mr. Freeman’s copious feet, and had been
starved, and struck in the face with the
butt end of a whip, and she had been
locked up in a damp cellar all through
a cold June night, along with her shivering
infant. Of all these things the divorce-law
took no notice whatsoever, and so far as it was
concerned the hob-nailed gentleman might have
pranced upon her till he grew too old to prance
any longer. But finally he fixed his affections
—which possibly meant his boots —upon some
other woman, and Judge Windeyer granted a
divorce upon the ground of adultery. The
divorce-law has a keen nose for this latter
offence, though of cruelty it takes no notice
whatever. Yet cruelty is, in every sense, the
worse offence of the two, and none save
a priest-ridden code could regard it other-
wise. A wrong that is unknown to the
injured party is practically no wrong at
all. Mr. Freeman or any other individual
might have committed adultery without his
injured partner being aware of the circum-
stance, and in that case her life would not be
one whit less happy for his backsliding ; but
he certainly could not jump on her with his
ponderous feet without her knowing the fact,
and suffering thereby. The injury concerning
which the law is so solicitous would assuredly
be a cruel one to any woman of a sensitive
order—if she chanced to become conscious of
it; the one which the law is not at all solicitous
about is cruel to any woman, and there is no
possibility of her finding refuge from it in ignor-
ance. The question at issue is not the moral or
snuffling aspect of the subject—simply regarded
as a struggle with the ten commandments, there
is as much reason why divorce should be granted
for a breach of one commandment as of any
other; the matter concerns exclusively the
suffering inflicted by one individual upon
another. Adultery does inflict suffering —pro-
bably in one case out of ten, the other nine not
being found out; boots applied to the head
inflict suffering every time. Assuming that the
adultery is found out, and allowing- for the fact
that the boots admit of no concealment, it is
even then only a question of which causes the
most pain, and the probability is that the
brutal assault is the worse of the two on all
occasions. The woman who suffers a moral
injury may forget her pain—in nine cases out
of ten she does ; the woman who is scarred
and disfigured and turned into a thing of
loathing never does. The present state of the
law arises partly out of Anglo-Saxon callous-
ness, and chiefly out of Anglo-Saxon hypocrisy.
The Church wants to hold marriage sacred
because divorce causes a falling-off in the
superstitious veneration for the parson’s
formula, and along with that there is an
inevitable falling-off in the takings. The
Church raised money for centuries on the
theory that marriage was a matter with which
it alone could interfere for good or evil, and
when the State interferes, either to make or
unmake marriages, then the Church gets into
the position of a prophet whose curse has
missed fire. It has asserted all along
that something # dreadful would happen to
anyone who attempted to undo its work—
nothing dreadful does happen, and thus
the priest is floored, and the bishop is bowled-
out, and a great deal of old reverence is lost.
And, therefore, the Pastor Smxffkins of the
period presents a. printed wail of remonstrance
to men like Mr. Freeman, and offers a cheap
tract to his miserable partner, and prays
for them both, and states through his nose as
before that those whom the Lord has joined no
man should put asunder.
Otago Witness thus sums up Salvationism :
The opposite poles of eeclesiastioism to-day are the
R.C. Church and the Salvation Army. Each of them
regards the other as a work of the devil. Yet they are
surprisingly alike. Each is a despotism ; each has its
Pope—(General Booth, it is to be noted, rather likes the
name “ Pone,” or “ Papa,” or “Generalhe asks, “what
does it matter?”);—each puts forth in the extreme form
the religion of authority; each exacts unquestioning
obedience. Which of the two has the stronger liking for
pomps and processions, vestments and ritual, it were
hard to say. The one is as old as the hills, the other is
the birth of yesterday, but for good, old-fashioned spiri-
tual autocracy of the mediaeval sort there isn’t a pin to
choose between them. If I wanted a religion to save me
the trouble of thinking, and keep me comfortably cock-
sure of everything, either would suit ; though on
grounds of taste I should be compelled to give the
preference to St. Joseph’s and Bishop Moran. The tim-
brels of the Hallelujah lasses are not to my liking, any
more than their poke-bonnets, or the “pious orgies” in
which they disport them. General Booth is an absolute
pope ; his whcle ecclesiastical system is summed up in
“ obey orders and ask no Questions.” Strange to say, his
people love to have it so. Talk about kissing the Pope’s
toe ! Is there a Salvationist in Dunedin who wouldn’t
think it an honour to lick the General’s boots? To people
who suppose that the religion of authority is played out
General Booth’s triumphal progress through the Austra-
lian colonies ought to be an eye-opener.

Someone signing himself “Henry Farmer”
writes in the Herald suggesting that the
lottery system should be adopted in all future
public loans in New South Wales, so that the
money may be raised in Australia instead of in
the English market as heretofore. The process
is exceedingly simple. The loan will be in, say,
£lO bonds, so as to be a handy means of invest-
ment, and thereby the Government will get the
money which now goes into shady banks,
bottomless mines, and totalisators run by
speculators with overhanging beaks and too
much jewellery. The interest will be fixed at
a low rate, and the loan, instead of falling due
in one lump, as at present, will be redeemed
by, say, 200 quarterly drawings—the bonds
which come out of the bag on each occasion

George Reid, the gentleman with the glass eye
and the happy smile, has been elected to suc-
ceed Parkes as leader of the New South Wales
Opposition. His first public act in his new
capacity was to wave the gory shirt of sectarian
hate, and to boil over concerning Dutch
William and the poor little ancient skirmish at
the Boyne. He raked up this battered legend
by the simple process of telling the Dices
Ministry that it ought not to rake it up, and
flourished the blood-stained rag of strife by
urging the other party not to flourish it. The
cause of his indignation was that Slattery, the
Roman Catholic, and Kidd, the Orangeman, sit
side by side in the Protectionist Cabinet. From
this fact it is evident that the Dibbs Government
does not intent to “travel on” religious rage, for
if it meant to trade on the Catholic block vote the
presence of Kidd would be fatal to it, and if
it intended to live on the support of the Orange
Party, Slattery would be a ruinous encum-
brance. It exists entirely on political issues,
and the fury of the politician with the vitreous
eye is roused purely because the new Cabinet
declines to live by howling about a dead and
gone row which took place between two foreign
nations at the other end of the globe some 200
years ago. Therefore Reid digs up the corpse
of the aged dissension—-which has not, and
never had, any more to do with Australia than
the wars of Jugurtiia and the Romans—and
while he shakes the venerable remains he howls
to his opponents not to shake them, and
at the same time he denounces them for
letting them rest. ' And now, we pre-
sume, the Evening News will take up the
parable as it did a few years ago, and
write another big curse (the last one was
written by a legal member of the present New
South Wales Opposition) because the Protec-
tionist Government is preserving an “ Ominous
Silence” on the subject of William lII.
which is much the same as complaining that it
is lying low on the topic of Julius Cjesar, and
isn’t making a really healthy row concerning one
Peter the Great, who used to have fits in Russia
about the early part of last century.
The cable-man on Carington :
The National Observer describes Lord Carington’s
recent speech at Bristol as “ blather.” While in S 3 dney,
it says, his indiscretions were concealed under a sporting-
exterior.
The Saturday Review ridicules the idea of Lord Caring-
ton pretending that the Conservatives are the foes of the
colonies, while the Liberals are their friends.
How much indiscretion can any man “conceal
under a sporting exterior ” ? and how does the
Observer suppose a Governor can hide his
political opinions by sending bashed footballers
home in Ids carriage ? Carington was a
failure; but when he had his sporting exterior
on he was as bad a failure as at any other time.
He was partly a swell-coachmqai gone wrong
and partly a clothes-stand and a wax image
out of a hairdresser’s window, and the only
time New South Wales ever wanted him for
any practical purpose—on the occasion of the
Mount Rennie case—it found him sitting up in
the latter capacity. It was a fine summer
morning, and Carington’s hair was looking
beautiful, and the hang of his new suit was
a joyful thing to contemplate, but the country
has now found that he was only hair and
clothes—that, apart from the “downiness”
which is acquired by every perky stable-boy,
he had no more brains than the average
barber’s clerk. He had one chance, and one
only, during his Governorship, to prove him-
self a manly Englishman who could uphold in
later days, and in the case of even the meanest
criminal who was yet a citizen over whom the
flag of his sovereign waved, the spirit of the
proud ancient boast, “ Civis Romanus sum /”
Yet he proved himself to be not a man, but a
mouse, not a great pro-consul with the true
kingly love of justice in his heart, but a nerve-
less popularity-hunter in whom the sense of
“British justice” was dead, or had not been
born—a man incapable of rising to a great
occasion and casting his official fortunes into the
scale alongside a principle worth dying for.
No, Charles Robert Baron Carington heard
not the voice of ideal British justice, but
only the brutal public’s frenzied bawl for the
blood of boys condemned to death without a
trial.
It is anything but unusual to read of con-
demned murderers eating and sleeping equably up
to the fatal morning when they are to meet their
mystery ; but it is seldom we hear of one so care-
ful of his health and so punctilious regarding his
meals as ttie late Maurice Dalton showed himself.
A week before his execution, finding himself
suffering from a cold, he insisted upon seeing the
gaol doctor, who prescribed a cough-mixture, and
he grumbled when taking his last breakfast that
the bread-and-butter was cut too thick for his
taste. One newspaper report says that Dalton
trembled at the moment the noose was being ad-
justed, but the fact is that Howard, who had
spent the night in oiling the rope, sent the knot
violently under the victim’s left ear and made him
stagger. He died with resolution and resignation,
and without making any statement, which is con-
sidered supererogatory by the R.C. Church after
a man has made bia peace with the Creator.

816 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Nov. 25, 1891.
M v Dkar Mooradinua,—
In the “ Corsair,” at Sydney Royal, Fanny Liddiard is
the most gorgeous millinery interpreter observable in a
Haunting production, where the tights of the chorus show
signs of being French-chalked at the knees to conceal the
tide-marks of a long Melbourne run. Pale-blue and tan,
also heliotrope and silver, show Fanny off to the best
advantage, and if the beauteous Conrad is almost in-
audible when he raises his voice in a piratical lay, it is a
fault shared by the rest of the company, who are all
vocally worn-out with long-continued squawking. Vir-
ginia Earle (Bernhardt saw her in “ Evangeline ” and pro-
nounced that she had “charm”—she said George
Anson had it, too) has bazaar-doll frocking as Medora;
her most artistic attire was white silk with borderings of
gold, worn in the harem scene; but, like the rest of the
rags, it bore evidence of having seen service. The night
I suffered through the performance, the d.c. was quite
“ musical ” —Mrs, Vanderveer Greene, in black, with terra-
cotta bars; and the Misses Albu, in, respectively, black
and gold and cobweb-coloured lace.
Speaking of the “ stage-warblers do you know the
Victorian damsel (it all happened ever so long ago, of
course), who became suddenly paralysed by an opera-
singer at the theatre one night? She was engaged to a
sound and reliable M.L.C., and the double-event was to
come off in a month’s time, when (as luck would have
it) she and her elderly-looking, ugly lover, and her
purring mama paid a visit to the opera. The baritone
of the show had scarcely taken his vocal preliminary ere
the fiancee began to reach for her opera-glasses, and
glare fixedly at the nice young man, and exhibit other
rash symptoms —but the climax was yet to come. As the
show drew to an end the lady drew off her glove;
wrenched off the big diamond-ring the M.L.C. had given
her, tied it to her bouquet with her little pocket-hand-
kerchief and flung ring and flowers at the feet of the
grease-paint hero. The M.L.C. didn’t wait to be given
his matrimonial congi after that, and the lady eventually
married somebody else—not her footlight fancy, of
course.
There seems to be a slightly economical aspect about
being chummy with the gallant officers of the fleet
moored round about Barataria, especially when you
have a nursery-full of boy bantlings whom it is
your fad to dress up d la Jack Tar. Because you
can pick up any sort of straw hat to top tlum
off with, and dear Captain So-and-So finds a silk
ribbon with the name of his ship on it—he has lots in th e
stores and obliges his lady friends whenever Tommy wears
the last one out. These hat-bands, badges of friendship
with Captain Corcoran, or even Sir Joseph Porter—are
gratefully paraded by the recipients thereof, who hug
themselves over the fact that the ship’s bands aren’t sold
in the shops, and the miles-away gaze with which the
proud mama of the fleet-branded boylet regards the pitiful
little outsider with “Spitfire” or some such shop-sold
designation round his skull is lovely to see. Vice-regal
peop’e aren’t above snapping in a band for their small
nautical hopeful either, and a society woman in Barataria
rather spreads her features over the fact that her brat and
the Guv’nah’s are the only two favoured with bands off
the swellest ship in the squadron. Also (but this is quite
a stage aside) I hear of a beauteous society blonde who fell
in love with an officer, and got him to give her a pair of
those broadcast ribbons with the name of his ship in bold
gold letters—and now' she uses them to—er—keep her
hose from wrinkling.
Under the fostering care of “Charlie” Huenerbein a
series of six chamber matinee concerts—a musical Phoenix
from the ashes of the deceased Orpheus Society—started
on their careerinSydney Town-hall vestibule, on 17th inst.,
in the presence of about a dozen paying patients with a
residuum of dead-heads—but the room was only half-full,
even then. Concerts of strictly classical construction
held at the leisurely hour of three p.m. at a charge of
three shillings per seat must run to seed in Sydney—the
whole idea is too dear and dilettante to get on otherwise
than backwards beside the Beautiful Harbour. Artisti-
cally regarded, the first concert was a success—the opening
string quartette (Beethoven) with Poussard, Schmellits’
check (the society fiddler), Strauss and Rice as inter,
preters, and Deslouis’ rendering of Gounod’s “ Le Soir,’-
were the most inspired items on the card. The tenor of
the occasion was a strange sight who sang Beethoven’s
“Adelaide” in a small, hysterical voice and balanced a
calf-bound song-book as I have seen nurses balance a
baby, also his articulation was very mumble-jumble, and
one was glad to see him go. Miss Niay, at the pianor
played in greased-lightning way, but her right hand is
often completely drowned by her left, and she shouldn’t
use her fists as if they were the forelegs of a nose-action
trotter—it spoils the music. By the way, it got so dark
in the hall (never very bright in the sunniest day) that
artificial light was needed, and great giggling was heard
on the appearance of Charles aforesaid with a “dip” in a
tin-candlestick!
Talking of Music, heavenly maid, reminds me that a
forty h.p. lady contralto over whom all Australia made
such a fuss—doesn’t return love for love although we-
did shower her with flattery. At the saloon-table,,
going back, I am told she would dilate at great
length on the “ pigs’ manners of the colonials,” and how-
“ you could always tell an Australian by the way he used'
his serviette,” which was only another instance of “ set a-
thief to catch a thief,” you know, for in Sydney, at least,,
when the great songstress had finished stoking, it was
her habit to glance furtively round to see that nobody
was noticing, and then dexterously use her first Angel-
as a tooth-pick ! People liked her at first, but soom
wearied of her parrot-like remark : “ I must tell that to-
the Prince of Wales,” and her “personal” anecdote*
about Royalty made them feel tired. In Japan, on the
way home, she gave a hen tea-party, and, during its pro-
gress, told the assembled women, “The Queen kissed me
on this cheek.” But great was the she-baritone’s rage
when somebody chipped in—“ I suppose you haven’t
washed the place since? ”
“ All Sydney ” was under canvas last Saturday evening
—at Sells’ circus in Moore Park, with a strong sprinkling
of society in the dress-circle. Judge Windeyer and
daughters had a front seat, and the air was darkened with
doctors. Sells’ circus is far from being a sell. You
missed the quarantined gee-gees, of course, and the lovely
Amazons in festive array (the one in moss-green plush
shouldn’t yank her skirt quite so tight to the back, by the
way) could only wander aimlessly round the track on
their own little feet, and the procession of Romans (on
foot) was an excising spectacle, which only served to draw
attention to the bow-legs cl the equestiians. Also, the
glittering tiiumphal cars (on one, a gold-washed card-
board Jove offered a paper cockatoo to a pasteboard
Minerva) rather missed fire by being hauled round the
track by badly-matched circus hands instead of rearing
nags—but these were unavoidable drawbacks, which will
be got over in a little while. I fell hopelessly in love with
Major and Mrs. Rae, two dear, little human dolls wit.;
delightful manners themost courlier-like Johnny could
model himself on the Majors bow, and “how do?”—and
then the shadow of the giantess fell over me, and I couldn’t
help thinking, as I gazed up at her eight-feet-two of
massive womanhood (she carries her great height very
gracefully!, what a charming chaperone she would make !
You could see her coming such a long way off ! The
hippopotami—resembling huge pigs in the body, with
hides like badly-cooked Xmas pudding—were all there,
but among the animals, “ Sid,” the trick elephant, must
take leading laurels. To see him waltz, and sit down, and
fan himself, and drink from a bottle, and use a serviette to
dry his mouth, is to set him up as the Bernhardt of the
entire company.
The circus ladies are very well got up—on cigarette-
photo. models, with frantic golden hair—and the acrobats
are skin-fitted in dazzling style—all white spangled with
silver, looking lovely in the lime-light. The Gilfort
Brothers (a man from ’Ome tells me that he saw them
posing 25 years ago in London, so it probably isn’t all
padding underneath their pipe-clayed Jerseys) were
dramatic and artistic in their living statuary acts ; and
the Circassian lady snake-charmer’s parrot-like attire —
green and red—went against the pit, from which a still,
small voice penetratingly remarked, “ Pretty cooky !”
The patriotic dander of billy-boiling Australia ought to
arise at the news that English folks smile in a superior,
we-know-better-how-to-do-it way at the Antipodean
method of making tea. As a matter of fact, you rarely
get well-made tea in England any more than you do in
the Australian boarding-house, where Mary Jane nurses
the teapot on to the table 20 minutes before the gong
goes, then sets a kettle (they call it a “samovar” in
London) of half-boiled waiter next to it for the missus to
temper the tannin with, and gleefully rings people down
to be poisoned. The Australian bushraan, however,
knows how to make tea, and if he does in his thirst con-
sume that which he should not consume, the fact is to be
attributed to his carelessness, not to his ignorance. Boil
a billy of water, and while it is still boiling on the
fire throw in the necessary quantity of tea, stir it rapidly
three or four times and take it off instantly ; wait till the
herb sinks half-way to the bottom, and then pour off the
water into your quart-pot, and you will have perfect tea.
Melbourne Austral Salon (they had drafted a programme
de reception, which almost included laurel-wreaths and
incense) prostrated itself before Rudyard Kipling, who
paid them the compliment of two visits—the last to an
afternoon tea, when a full attendance resulted. Mr-
Kipling is described to me as “decidedly good-looking, and
below middle height, with well-developed chest and
shoulders.” He talks “ rapidly and incessantly, and has
the laugh of a schoolboy, and the assured manner of a
man who has been made much of by women.” lam per-
versely glad to hear that an artisth member of the salon,
who doesn’t keep her weather-eye on current literature >
didn’t quite “pick up her moorings,” so to speak, for on
being introduced to the god of the occasion, she peered
up into his face and said—“Do I know you? Have we
met before?” and then, turning to another member, asked
audibly, “ Who is he? What are they making such a fuss
over him for?” R. Kipling rather liked the onslaught of
beauty and genius, but two or three brothers of members,
who lent a hand in the hero-worship, got a rather hike,
warm reception. Later on, at the Yorick Club, the novelist
is reported to have given forth this rather cheap common-
place : “ I don’t mind being surrounded to the verge of
suffocation by a lot of good-looking women” (R.K. is
henceforth quite certain of his Melbourne readers), “ but I
strongly object to being loved by their little brothers.”
Sydney Women’s Literary Club let Rudyard K. “ slide.’
The S.W.L.C. is a small and lonely aggregate which wants
galvanizing badly. It is an abridged and bowdlerised
institution which declines to walk on the same side of the
street as George Eliot, and which wouldn’t invite Annie
Besant or “Ouida” (should they chance on Sydney) to
one of their dreary conventionals, because the former has
aired such improper topics, and Mrs. B. has said such
awful things. Nothing more excitingly literary than a
latter-day Doctor Watts or Maria Edgeworth would ever
be chased by this institution, and I hear that a member
wished she had died rather than done reverence to the
imminent Max O’Rell. I wonder how the pretty members
like the lines on which the Sydney Women’s Literary
Institution is conducted!
A story is told of how Rudyard Kipling’s frequently
abstracted gaze was misread by an irreverent young
lady on board the steamer. She was sitting next to the
litterateur at dinner one evening, and noticing him drop
his knife and fork, and begin glaring absently at his pro-
vender, she jogged his elbow and asked him in an anxious
whisper: “Goodness! is it a hair, or a fly, or what?”
R.K. ceased cogitating and went on with his masticating.
The latest addition to local melody is the “Cardross”
Valse, by John de S. Mann, of Sydney, who presents it to
an admiring world in a mulberry cover and five flats.
Nobody could whistle the music, but the one-two-three
is well-sustained, and if the ground-plan, as it were, of
the composition is reminiscent of boiler-making at a
distance, nobody who dances to it will have to put four
steps in the bar. .

The gentlemen who sat on the tables at N.S.W. Govern-
jnent House garden-party on Friday afternoon, with
their legs hanging found like a fringe, who sprawled on
the lawn smoking cutty pipes, while their better halves
investigated the drawers in the sideboards in their
rambles through' the vice-regal residence, probably
looked on the gubernatorial mansion as a public institu-
tion on inspection-day. lady who, not content with
a square feed on the took homewards in her
umbrella a bottle of— well, it wasn’t sarsaparilla—and
some cakes for the twins and Johnny, must have felt sad
when that striped handkerchief which she had hastily
used to secure her possessions in her umbrella came loose
and her adieux were cut short by a scatter of provisions
before the company. In future a large card, “Eat
all, but pocket none,” will adorn the verandahs at Sydney
Government House garden-parlies.
Doesn’t it amuse you, in an insolvent community
where one man in every ten finds even one cheap wify
miles beyond his means, to hear them joke and grig,
when the marriage-a-frost shriek rises on the breeze
about the expediency of polygamy to settle the woman
question ? Most of them couldn’t afford to embrace
Mormonism and two wives even if they wanted to! Xhe
“polygamy" jest runs on level lines with the “cock-
sureness” of the frayed bank-clerk on fifty- shillings
weekly, who solemnly tells you that he hasn’t quit e
made up his mind whether he will marry ornot.

The “ Orlando ” dance on 17th instant was the brightest
spot in a yawning week, which was only roused by that
and the Government House garden-party to Parlia-
mentary, mayoral and aldermanic wights with their
lady relatives, who strayed about the lawn to the
strains of the P.A. band for an hour or two, and dis-
persed at 6 p.m. filled with loyalty, and tea, and gratifica-
tion. The other event—the “ Orlando ” dance—although
it rained Egeson s delight on the mystic date-got off very
well in spite of the fact that Governor Jersey didn’t go
owing to the falling water, and that the frocking of the
women present (headed by Lady Charles Scott) presented
a foundered appearance round the hems. The warlike
vessel (some people object to her on social occasions, since
the flagship is for use, and sassiety bumps its bunions
against the big guns in the mazy waltz) was disguised in
flowers; the music was good, and the hop was a success.
Musical people on tour have to face queer houses some-
times. Flautist Lemmone, just back from a provincial
raid, tells of one “ picturesque ” audience of rabbiters in
gore-stained garments, and fresh-caught “ bullockies’’
who, moved by the rolling rhythm, rose and cracked their
whips together in time to the music ! At one way-back
one-night township the man in front was accosted by a
small, touzled, barefooted specimen of bush-gal who
wanted to get in on the nod, “ because I loves to hear ’em
singing, mister, an’ I’m stone-broke!” She had a front
seat.

manian scandal wanted to maqry the young lady

A man used to lecture in Melbourne,
proving that all the next generation must be
teetotallers. But his widow kept a hotel.
A rather startling, but Pharisaically-logical
social development has occurred as the result of
the paper read, by a now well-advertised lady, at
a recent meeting of the N.S.W. Woman’s Suffrage
Association.
Tom Westbrook, the Hobart auctioneer,
suggests in the press, that a day be set apart for
national prayer, to avert influenza, typhoid, etc,
A local clergyman scouts the idea in favour of a
back-yard-cleaning campaign.
It is cabled that Sir George Grey “is
likely to be appointed to the vacant governorship
of New Zealand.” What next will the cableman
ask us to swallow? Why, the crowd which
refused to Higinbotham the Victorian Lieutenant-
Governorship are still in power.
Why can't the police catch A. H. M‘Cul-
loch, Esq., solicitor, late of Sydney, whom so
many people want to see so badly, and who is
still in Australia? A, H. M'Culloch, Esq., is, it
■is true, high up in a certain secret organisation,
but surely there must be a few people “ not in the
ring.”
A recently-elected N.S.W. member of
Parliament, the other day, appeared in the local
halls of legislation with a pipe in his mouth.
Were a vote taken as to smoking in the Chamber
it is quite likely that the “ayes’ would have
many votes. They smoke during debates at the
Trades and Labour Council.
Bell and Davis managed to “ slew ” the
post-office authorities in Sydney and San Fran-
ciso ; ergo they “ slewed ” the detectives. The
detectives, when they want to catch you, first
enquire: “ Who is his girl?” and secondly,
“ Where will his letters be addressed ? ” If they
can’t answer either question they are as helpless as
cataleptic infants.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co’s. “Cascade”
80 & 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
