Under ordinary circumstances a hippo-
without horses as a place of amusement
flro 'id be as diverting as Sheol after Satan had
bppn shelved on a retiring-allowance. But your
.id newspaper man is always equal to every
Irnpra-ency, and Editor Stow is prepared to run
onreessfuily Sells’ Bros. Circus on stilts should no
S ther adjuncts to locomotion be obtainable.
Therefore is the mammoth show at Moore Park a
beam in the eyes of theatrical managers and a
Brobdingnagian blister on their treasuries. Moat
f Sells’wild beasts don’t shine brightly enough
m irive sunstroke when contemplated severely.
Y o ur much-travelled lion is usually only a fourth-
Ate door-mat—but their hippopotami are hogs of
hiffh degree, and their performing elephants, five
in number, are things to shed tears of joy over.
It is impossible to view critically three circus
displays in three rings at one time without
losing an eyeball or even two, and so
Colonel Sellars might with advantage lay on
an oculist in the interests of suffering journalism.
So far as two eyes were permitted to observe, out
of 100 performers—wire-walkers, pole-climbers,
weight-lifters, attitudinisers, trapezists, giants,
dwarfs, air-jumpers, bar-athletes, contortionists,
cyclists, gymnasts, jugglers, tumblers, Japanese,
and Arabs—Sells haven’t got a duffer. Best to
bur mind are the Silbons, Stirks, Costellos (on
horizontal bars) Rex and Reno, simply wonderful
contortionists, the Gilforts, as Grecian statues,
and some of the Arab and Jap oerformers. As
for the “ female giantess ” (see bills) she looks at
least eight feet high and every inch a lady; unlike
ordinary giantesses—usually mannish creatures—
she has no moustache and a fine pectoral develop-
ment. But the bearded lady has hair enough for
a mattress-factory, and the lady serpent-charmer
handles cobra and anaconda as carelessly as if
they were indiarubber piping. We have slain
many circus-clowns in bur time—the sharks of
Sydney Harbour are simply choked with their
bones—but we have left Dan Ryan off our black
list; he is funny.
** * *
“The Little Widow,” a farcical comedy by
the English author, F. Jarman, now running at
Sydney Garrick, is, on the whole, amusing,
especially in the second act. Wilkins Parker (H.
Shine), an amateur mesmerist, having put a
“ hawful hugly ” female “ under the influence ” at
a public meeting, is unable afterwards to get rid
of her. At the same time “the little widow”
(Jennie Lee) comes on a self-invited visit to
Wilkins’ brother Arthur (Frank Cates), and the
situation is complicated by the arrival of Mrs.
Parker (Constance Deorwyn) and Arthur’s fiancee,
Emily. The fun then arises out of the necessity
for Jceeping the foreign woman out of sight.
H. Shine, Jennie Lee, and Frank Cates, in the
order named, have most of the “ fat,” but the
performance is that of G. P. Carey, whose in-
dividuality is completely merged in that of the
wife-hunting restaurateur. The “hawful hugly
’un,” with the stony stare, the large mouth, and
insatiable appetite, is admirably conceived and
carried out by “our Mary Ann.” Jennie Lee is,
of course, bright and lively as Constance Rattle-
brain, and Shine’s is not a bad performance. “Jo”
will probably go up on Saturday night.
** * *
“ Schoolmistress,” after eight months’
vacation, has proved profitable at Sydney Cri-
terion during its six nights’ run. The manage-
ment have accepted The Bulletin’s last season
hint, and Maggie Moore now, by way of rehearsal,
lights up the first act with a waltz song of the
minstrel type. The little lady’s best effort is in
the long denunciation of her husband. That blue-
blooded pauper is again ably handled by Dot
Boucicault, now scarcely so much collar-throttled
as previously. Titheradge by no means fills the
sea-boots of Admiral Rankling, formerly stretched
to their utmost by Anson. Miss Romer, as the
down-trodden worm of the naval bully, repeats a
former success, as do Percy Lyndal, Chichester,
Bessie Mayor, and Master Whyte. Miss Spong,
as Mrs. Paulwer, goes up one into the wed-
ding - slippers vacated by Emma Temple, and
Mr. Watson is too large and lubberly as
Tyler, the page, G. L. Gordon, as the German
composer, Bernstein, does not lose by comparison
with his predecessor, Mr. Clitherow. “Pick-
pocket” goes up on Saturday, and on sth Decem-
ber Williamson’s Juvenile Opera Co. open in
“The Mikado.” J.C.W. seems to have temporarily
deserted the Castlereagh-street theatres.
#* * *
The art of modern play-acting is to amuse.
Therefore, to those who laugh heartily, enquiry
into the reasons for their mirth is not necessary.
Their cachinnation serves as the laughter-giver’s
defender and best apology. “ The Corsair,” being
built on similar lines to “ Cinder-Ellen ” et hoc
genus omne, the Sydney audiences that lately
laughed so consumedly at the latter show cannot
consistently cavil at the former. Business has,
despite prevailing depression, been unvaryingly
good with “The Corsair,” but the event of the
season so far has been the gallery demon-
stration of the third night. On that occa-
sion the fair and fat Fortescue was singing
that most inane of all music-hall ditties,
“For Goodness Sake don’t say I told You,” and,
enthusiastically called, re-called, and re-re-called
for “The Man in the Moon,” kept repeating,
“For Goodness Sake,” &c., till he could scarcely
wheeze. Then he suddenly stopped, while the
“ gods,” or rather a rowdy section of these uppish
folks, kept on whistling, yelling, and tramping.
James MacMahpn came on but wasn’t heard,
and after the omission of several items, and at a
period when half the circle and two or three rows
of stalls had filed out, the show was allowed to go
on. Now, the management announce that the
Theatre Royal is not stage-managed from the
gallery, and that “Man in the Moon” belongs to
“Evangeline” and will only be sung when that
musical heifer occupies the stage,
* * # *
“Youth” holds its own at Sydney Her
Majesty’s during a period of theatrical dearth and
disaster unprecedented in the memories of local
managers. Dress-circle folks always have nerves,
and as the sudden banging of 100 guns is a shock-
producer to which smelling-salts is a poor antidote,
why, the bongtong don’t exactly froth over the
plush-covered barriers into the stalls. But the
house otherwise is fairly filled, and this play, the
joint production of Sir “Gussy” Harris, Pettitt
and Merrit, may hold the fort until Santa Claus
comes along with “ The Babes in the Wood,” and
Maggie Moore again in the lead. If it doesn’t,
Mr. Rignold has got something good up his sleeve.
* * * *
It is rather late in the day, but neverthe-
less the fact, painful though it be, must be
chronicled. Manager Beresford, of the Cyclorama,
has been compelled to retire on account of the
prevailing malady, and a gentleman named Bogie
reigns in his stead. The Oyclorama’s orchestra
has, therefore, been compelled to drop one musical
item from its programme.
* * * *
Manager Wyburd bubbles over with joy,
for the plate-glass for his fish-tanks has arrived,
and all Sydney may soon see at Bondi what it can
never hope to see at breakfast time — i.e., fresh
fish. Meantime, Sylvo, one of the smartest pre-
stidigitateurs extant, and other good men are
showing there.
* * * *
Now that the hot summer has begun, and
the Australian is filled with a great longing to
look at the cool, green sea, Coogee Aquarium is
booming violently. Manager Larmour has taken
a lease of those sweet singers, the Misses Albu,
also the Elite Quartette out of the “ Corsair,” and
in consequence the success of his Sunday con-
certs is something large. Also, there are all
manner of other attractions, including monsters of
the deep, and cool green spots, and refreshing
drinks, and lovely scenery ad lib, and, therefore,
the Coogee Palace is full while the churches are
emptv.
* * * *
“ Rob Roy ” is running a second week at
Melbourne Royal, and a score of supers in naked
knock-knees continue to make a dismal pretence
of staining their native heath with Sassenach
gore. They are a very job-lot of outlaws, these
followers of the bold Macgregor. A wild life
amongst the hills and crags of Bonnie Scotland
probably accounts for their ill and scraggy appear-
ance, or, perhaps, they have become lean and
leaden-eyed through croaking bacchanalian
choruses when they are quaffing nothing out of
empty drinking-cups. One might suggest a whole
heap of reasons why these ruthless marauders
should look so crushed. For instance, they can’t
help noticing that Rob and Mrs. Mac. are trying
to palm off upon them a couple of slips of girls as
a pair of sturdy sons. Desperate men don’t like
to have their intelligence insulted in this manner,
yet they are expected to stand around and beam
with pride whilst their gallant chieftain pitches
such yarns concerning the brawn and muscle of his
bogus hoys that it’s a wonder he doesn’t blow them
into the footlights. The spectacular scenes of “Rob
Roy ” are ruined by the feeble presence of the
Macgregors junior, who ought to retire from law-
less life, and take to darning their pa’s socks.
Fortunately there is something to admire in the
aspect of Bentley, who now plays the name-part
with a little more breadth of style than at the
outset, and is manly and natural all the time.
Eloise Juno, as the raging mother of Helen’s
babies, is not so natural, yet she also is manly—
moreover, she is careful, like her chieftain, to
avoid chipping in with a tune at unexpected
moments. But the Macgregors are little more
than ornamental features of the production, after
all. Cathcart and Armes Beaumont almost
mononolise the foreground, in turns, or both
together, and the entertainment is mostly wob-
bling between old comedy and ballad opera.
• # *
Cathcarts sound though stagey performance
of Nicol Jarvie deserves a good deal of the favour
it meets with, and although Armes Beaumont
doesn’t score heavily as a dashing gallant and
serious stage - lover, he preserves the musical
portion of the trouble from unmitigated contempt,
A Mr. George Manson, as the Dougal Creature,
gives an apparently faithful representation of that
uncombed heathen, and Albert Bernard gets some
fun out of his Major Galbraith. Alderson, the
evil Rashleigh Osbaldistone (a cunning chiel),
conveys the notion of a pirate trying to crouch
into his big boots, and most of the others are
subjects too painful for discussion. “ Rob Roy,”
which is creditably mounted, has a reasonable
chance, at time of writing, of hanging on for a
few nights longer, but “ Hamlet” is in rehearsal.
* * * *
A revival of “The Gondoliers” caused
the Princess’ to fill right up on Saturday night.
Custom doesn’t stale the music and humour of
Gilbert and Sullivan’s latest (and possibly last)
opera—nay, the managerial declaration that it
grows upon the understanding and improves upon
acquaintance is a plain truth. Shortcomings in
the cast are even more noticeable than of yore,
however. When a merry maiden marries she is
usually a few feet taller than Flora Graupner, or
Violet Varley. The substitutions of shrinking
Violet for Ida Osborne, and of tall Bracy for
Leumane, bring urgent necessity for full-grown,
graceful lady principals prominently before the
public. The former standard of vocalism is more
than maintained, though. Fair Florence Young
improves each shining hour, and ; the valuable
voice of the new contralto, Opie, calls
attention to the momentary apparition of the
mother of Barataria’s lawful sovereign.
* * * *
Melbourne Alexandra re-opened on Satur-
day with a new nightmare of local interest,
hatched by the heated imaginations of Dampier
and Garnet Walch, and entitled “ This Great
City.” The accent is on the This, because Andrew
Halliday once wrote a drama called “ The Great
City,” in which Madge Robertson made her Lon-
don debfit, and was abducted by the dingy agents
of a cronk financier, much as Lily Dampier is
taken captive in the lurid narrative under notice.
Otherwise there is no resemblance between the
two dramas. Melbourne seems to be deeper sunk
in crime and richer in howling lunacy than the
London of poor Halliday’s time. Most of the
Dampier-Waloh creations are bent on destruction,
and they can’t fix their horrid gaze upon the
casual stranger for ten seconds without wanting to
east him into eternity and purloin his “ papers.”
Women seem to them as bits of portable property,
to be picked up by the way and carried to a place
of safety, for fear the poor things might get wind
of the stolen will and do something that would
burst up the boss villain and ruin all.
# * *
The exact plot of “ This Great City ” is
generally lost in a murky atmosphere of crime,
but as far as can be gathered in the darkness
there is a missing document at the root of the
matter, and if a mild young villain in a sham
beard could but contrive to find this paper and
marry the girl, then a lot of other miscreants
might go whacks in the spoil, or something of that
sort. The trouble is to discover which of the two
Dampier damsels he has concentrated his wicked
eye on, because there’s a more elderly monster,
who seems to' want the other, and when a couple
of hired abductors prowl into the young ladies’
humble garret, remarking, “ It’s the fair one
we’ve got to take,” and try to take Lily
(who is a dark one) the plot thickens worse
than before. Nothing is ever certain except
the providential arrival of Alfred Dampier
at the right time, and the opportune
assistance of Mrs. D., who is well-suited
with a low-comedy part. Satisfactorily played in
the popular Alexandra style, there was no doubt
about “ This Great City ” catching on. Its local
allusions kept a large, excited audience in roars of
glee, and each stage-picture of an old familiar
spot, notably Sandriclge Pier by Night, went
home to a couple of thousand ’onest ’arts. The
Alexandra is a little world in itself, always worth
visiting when the Dampiers are in possession, and
the masses get a fair show to fire off their
feelings.
** * *
“Doctor Bill,” revived for a short season,
drew another big crowd to the Bijou. One or
two minor changes from the previous Melbourne
cast are improvements rather than otherwise, and
the social disturbances brought about by Anson’s
unhappy meddling with that extinct volcano, his
son-in-law, are excruciatingly funny to such as
haven’t already seen the riot. And although the
kangaroo dance has lost much of its early bloom,
and the hunt for the lost door-key is less of a
genuine novelty than aforetime, “ Dr. Bill ” ought
to prove a successful nerves-soother until Decem-
ber sth, after which date Myra Kemble takes her
medical adviser for a tour through Maoriland.
** * *
The halls and other perennial shows are
doing fairly well, or more so, but the desire to
hear more about Stanley and his heroism was not
strong enough to burst the Town-hall walls
asunder last Saturday. His two farewell sermons
w w to begin in the Athenaeum on Monday and
Tuesday.
** * *
George Darrell didn’t appear at Melbourne
Opera House on the date Originally mentioned.
The theatre has been shut up of late, and there
was an anxious period of doubt as to whether
George would be able to appear at all. It was a
terrible time for patrons of the drama, but all
promises to go well with them now, as George is
definitely announced to start the job this Satur-
day (28th). On that occasion the author-actor
purposes to submit “ Hue and Cry ” to the Critics
and Playgoers of Melbourne for Approval or Con-
demnation prior to its Presentation in England.
From which it would appear that if Melbourne
were to condemn the Darrellian effort with a
cartload of bricks, or a ton of bananas, he would
give the Britishers a chance to fall down and w***’-
ship it, all the same.
** * *
Gerald and Duff are not likely to make a
fortune over their dramatic season at the Ade-
laide Royal. This, however, is not altogether
surprising, as their dramas up to date are not
very wholesome, and, with one or two exceptions,
the company is decidedly frail. “The Points-
man,” with its railway collision and other
harrowing incidents, was not a great draw,
and The Bulletin passes over with contempt
the production of “ East Lynne.” Also, it hereby
offers a reward to the man, woman, or child who
will slay every member of the next company
that places this play before an already too
persecuted public. “The Silver King” is now
being tried. At the Town Hall, Snazelle has
finished up what should prove a remunerative
season. As the excellence of his entertainment
became known business improved rapidly. The
story of “ Enoch Arden,” with beautiful realistic
pictures illustrating the various scenes, was a big
attraction.
At Poverty Point.
From one of the Southern coffee - palaces conies a
pretty story of propriety avenged. The heroine, an
alleged artiste of a certain comic opera company, had
stooped to conquer the “ boots,” and that fortunate
nobleman’s neglect to rise with the lark and put the usual
polish on ’em led to a managerial discovery of a delicate
nature. The first virtuous impulse of the management
was to make a shocking example of the consenting syren
by requesting her to withdraw. Lodgers are lodgers,
however, in these hard times, and woman is generally-
admitted to be the weaker vessel. It was ultimately
decided that the difficulty could be got over, in a
respectable business-like way, by “ sacking ” that miser-
able profligate the “ boots,” and letting his lively
“ victim ” stay on.
When the father of the late Peter Mansergh, the
(Melbourne) Irish comedian, waited upon old Billy
Hoskins to negotiate a professional engagement for his
son, Billy’s first question settled the matter. “ Can your
son eat sawdust without butter ? If he can’t there is no
use in trying the stage,” Peter was more or less a
failure.
Perhaps in the whole of Australia there is no better-
known theatrical than Jimmy South, and the veteran
is copper-fastened throughout and, like Parkes’ “ Ragged
Jane,” “what he don’t know is not worth knowing.”
When Jimmy sued Carroll and wife (Emma Wangenheim)
in Brisbane recently, South’s own lawyer (Preske) had
got along with the examination in chief of his client to—
“ And this Emma Wangenheim is the defendant Carroll’s
wife?” “Yes”—-when up sprang Lawyer Rutledge to
score a point. “How do you know she is his wife?”
“ Wuw—well,” says Jimmy, “I know'it.” “ You know
it, sir ? How do you know the female defendant is Mr.
Carroll’s wife, sir? Now, you’re on your oath, sir!”
“ Well, I ought to know it, sir. Why, I gave her away in
marriage.” Then Rutledge sat down with a thud that
shook the court.
Mine. Fanuitza, the well-known and fetching “ Witch
of Atlas,” has turned up again in Sydney after two years’
visit to Europe.
The burly, massive Sala, who is not wholly unlike the
great and blubbery G.A.S., is again to the fore with some
petrifactions from romantic Cow Flat. He claims to have
discovered a second marble man, who this time is in a
crouching position, like the Listening Slave ; also a horse,
a prehistoric elephant, several snakes and lizards, with an
enormous fish, “ very like a whale." Sala has begun to
theorise learnedly, and he ’spects he has lighted on the
bed of a cave, where a federal council of antediluvian
creatures was held. At one time he purposed putting a
shot into the elephant’s trunk, so as to convince the
sceptics, but finally decided to have the monsters photoed.
He adheres to his statements with the equanimity of Don
Quixote after he had been in the cave of Montesinos, and
on the whole he is much more interesting than theSku-
thorpes. Pygmalion Sala sticks to the authenticity of
the Original Marble Man.

Here is an anecdote related by an Irish comedian of an
experience he once had in the Isle of Wight. The place
was Yentnor, and the prospects of a good “house ” were
dark. It occurred to the Hibernian artist to inquire if
there was an Irish quarter in the town, so he said to his
host, a hotel-keeper, “ Have you many Irish here ?” “ No,”
was the ingenuous reply, “ we are remarkably free from
them !” S. F. Argonaut.
Myra Kemble, with “Dr. Bill,” struck a N.Q. town
recently. She found herself unknown, and one inhabitant
asked the manager—" ’As she ever played with Dan
Barry ?” “No.” “Then she ain't no manner o’good.”
And the mirthful Myra fled from the settlement.
Myra Kemble tells with gusto this story. She was at
Armidale, N.S.W., enjoying the balmy twilight on the
balcony of her hotel. Suddenly she awoke to find herself
famous by means of the town-crier ! Seated on a sorry-
looking Rosinante, the ceaseless clatter in whose ears
apparently induced a slumberous meditation, the crier
authoritatively rang his bell and announced, “ To-night 1
to-night! to-night! Roll up, roll up, roll up, all of yez 1
Miss Maria Campbell, acknowledged the bow idol of her
perfeshun, will produce ‘Dr. Bill,’ with all the original
limelight effects, and 14 stock star artists at the back of
her! Also, this afternoon, 100 prime wethers, and 100
bullocks rolling fat, 5 pigs,” &c., &c., &c.

It w r as the shallowest of devices to advertise Walter
Bentley as “ the famous Scotch tragedian,” simply because
he was making his first appearance in “Rob Roy.” The
public will naturally associate him in their minds with an
everlasting Scotch accent, and be disposed to take it for
granted that for characters like Hamlet he might just as
well be Grattan Biggs.

I had the pleasure of meeting Miss Ada Behan last
w’eek (writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent). She
is dying (she says) to pay a visit to Australia, of which she
has heard so much; and she hopes, with the help of John
Drew, who is equally anxious to see Kangarooland, to
induce Daly to try a venture there. Miss Behan is quite
fascinated with the Poet Laureate, and intends to work
her hardest to make his “Maid Marian” a success.
Tennyson has made several important alterations in the
leading part to suit her.
Melbourne shareholders in the original “Waterloo”
Cyclorama Company have, in a few instances, sold out
and bought fresh shares in “The Siege of Paris.” Both
shows are being run by the same company, a fact which
doesn’t appear to be clearly understood on ’Change, or by
stock and share reporters for the dailies. The Argus, for
example, gravely stated that “Waterloos” had fallen on
account of the popularity of “ The Siege.”

as long as “Pinafore” does. When “Pinafore” is for-
gotten, no one will even remember that there was any
Smith.

Yet another Tasmanian veteran, Francis
Sherwood, aged 87, died recently at Hobart. He
came out under engagement to the old V.D.L.
Company in 1820, and was the pioneer of the
beautiful Huon district now known as the “ apple
orchai’d of the South.”

WHY HE SMILED.
DOCTOR : “How long has your husband been like this, madam ?”
ANXOUS WIFE : “He came home at lunch-time, apparently as usual, and told me he had
bought a new novel—that the story was laid in England, the principal here being
a retired Queenslander who had made his fortune by farming in Queensland.
Then he threw himself on the sofa and has never stopped laughing since.”
Which reminds one of what G. R. Sims lately
said when taunted with being a Jew : “ All
clever men are more or less Jewish.”
Some descriptions of the brilliant and
brilliantly red-headed poet, Swinburne, are going
the rounds of the papers. We saw him once in
London. His rufous hair excepted, he is the
image, on a smaller scale, of Henry Kendall, who
was dark-haired. The woebegonencss which was
the Australian poet’s distinctive expression is all
there.

A Mr; H. J, Merriman, in Lloyds, tells
another story of how Nelson died. Forty years
ago he frequently came in contact with a Tra-
falgar veteran, named Williams, who had been
Nelson’s cabin-boy, and who described the A dmiral
as a martinet disliked by his men in general. He
says that Nelson was shot by one of his own top-
men in revenge for harsh treatment.
The late Edward Deas-Thomson, before his
knighthood, was a director at Sydney of the
Liverpool and London and Globe Insurance Co.,
of which Alfred Stanger-Leathes was secretary.
At a board meeting one day, Mr. Thomson said,
“Mr. Leathes —” “I beg your pardon, Mi'.
Thomson, my name is Stanger-Leathes.” “ And
mine,” quoth the ex-imperialist, “is Deas-
Thomson.”
Mr. Sharp, of Newcastle, N.S.W., can
neither hear nor speak, but if you face him, at
any reasonable distance, and write in the air with
your finger, he will read the inverted and
invisible hieroglyphics with the utmost ease. He
will also tell you what you write on his back.
He will watcli you writing, from the front, with
only the top of your pen exposed to view, and tell
you what you have written. He exerts this
power with the utmost rapidity and with no more
difficulty than the ordinary reader who watches
the penmanship.
When Johnel Sullivan was in Sydney he
made up his mind every morning that he was not
going to drink too much. So he would go to the
bar of the hotel where ho stayed and, taking up
the smallest glass lie could see on the counter,
would say to the barmaid: “Now, little gel,
you’ll ’ave to look after me to-day. This is my
glass, little gel.” And all through the day John
used to pour in to and out of that little glass the
contents of any number of fizz bottles. Thus doth
the strong man deceive himself.
Colonel Olcotb’s religion, new in theory,
seems quite old in practice, judging from a lady’s
letter to a friend in Sydney. The lady was a
fellow-passenger on the Massilia, in which tire
Blavatsky graduate took passage from these
shores : —“ The dear old man is gallant (spoken
with a Byronic curl of the lip); he used to squeeze
our hands in a decidedly non-paternal way ; in
fact, had we not known that he was Colonel
Olcott, of warranted idealistic aspirations, his
palming work would have made him answer to
our conception of the modest Turk.”
C. D. Keane, P.M. and Mining-Registrar,
who died the other day in a N.Q. Hospital, was
well-known on all the Australian goldfields.
Keane, a tall, soldierly man, when in Ireland
years ago took an active part in the old Fenian
movement, and spent many days and nights in
drilling rebels. Ho used to boast that he had
been 14 times in gaol in his native country, which
he left disgusted, and spent the remainder of his
life in studying for the Bar, fossicking for gold,
and administering justice in Australia.'
During the sitting of the Parnell Commis-
sion the Irish leader frequently came into Court
carrying a small black bag, which, in course of
time, came to be quite celebrated, and much won-
der was expressed as to the nature of its contents.
Despite Parnell’s vigilance and tenacity the bag
was lost one day, and it turned up at Scotland
Yard. It was there opened—possibly with some
trepidation, certainly with considerable curiosity
—and was found to contain a pair of woollen
socks, which the dreaded owner used to carry
about in case his'feet got wet.
The Due D’Orleans, hero of the Melba
scandal, is described as “tall, slender, and of
sallow complexion,” resembling his grand-sire, the
old Due D’Orleans. The present Orleans’father,
the Comte do Paris, once declined to dine with
the Prince of Wales. “It is difficult to imagine
the Comte in ouch a circle,” says Count Paul
Vasili in his “ Society in Paris” : “ The exquisite
correctness of his manners, his concern for
morality, the elevation of thought and propriety,
which latter feeling is to him as a second nature,
could not associate with it.” Now comes Tommy's
turn to chuckle over the morals of the Orleans’
family.

the London millionaire furniture-man and race'

shabby in appearance, but Maple, being a brand-
new aristocrat, lias a monkey-soap polish on him
all the tune, and is hall-marked 24 carat. It is
related by a London paper that he met, at the
Derby, the Marquis of Ailesbury (in a shabbv
coat and a bad hat) driving with a lot of frowsy
theatncal women in an old, omnibus-shaped
shandridan. Hello, Ailesbury,” cried Maple
where does that ’bus of yours run ?” “ Runs to
iottenham Court-road. Let you down at your
shop if you like,” replied Ailesbury.

Our dear old friend General Mclver, the
rampant warrior who served “ under fourteen
flags,” turned up lately in New York, where he
told a solemn tale to, or wrote a tale for, a re-
porter, and the reporter put it in the paper.
Mclver, it seems, had drifted to Costa Rica,
where he was appointed Commander-in-Chief of
the army. _ He was also going to be Minister of
'v ar, and in course of time he would probably
have been the entire Cabinet and possibly several
dukes and the president as well, only the “native
aristocracy ” got up on its local black ear and
raised a row. A gigantic potentate named Cas-
tmez, who seems to have been eight feet high,
with double joints and a huge set of teeth, chal-
lenged Mclver to single combat, and they met in
a gory encounter with axes or something, and in
twenty seconds Castinez’s blood was spouting up
to the blue arch above. When he recovered he
came round by night with five savage homicides
all armed to the teeth, and raised Cain at the
General’s door. Then Mclver, by bis own account,
rushed forth and buried his shining blade in the
foremost brigand, and hove his mangled remains
at the heads of his accomplices. After this ex-
hibition the General fought the other five off and
on all through the night, killing three of them,
and badly discouraging another one, and in the
morning the street looked like a butcher’s em-
porium, and every house thereabouts was spattered
with brains. MTver resigned after this, receiving
a large sum of money and a sword of honour from
the grateful republic, and came to New York—to
write up the proceedings in the newspapers. This
is his own version of the case, and as The Bllle-
TIN never saw MTver howling on the bloodstained
field of battle, or charging in a hollow square with
the plunder in the centre, it takes the General at
his own valuation. The only time, in fact, that
this paper ever saw that hero in action was when
he was contending with an infant in a London
police-court. On that occasion the infant knocked
him out in one round, and lie had to pay ss. a
week.

Before the century
closes, the U.S. national
debt will have been ex-
tinguished. In 1870 it
was 2,386,000,000 dol-
lars ; in 1890 it was
988,000,000 dollars. The
per capita debt of the
U.S. is less than that of
any other nation —2B
dollars. It is 218 dollars
in France, and 100 dol-
lars in Great Britain.
The Playford Go-
vernment’s taxation
policy has gone hope-
lessly wrong, as most
people anticipated.
It comprised what
Playford called “ four
steps,” each step being
worse than the one before. The whole business
was simply a bold but unsuccessful attempt on
the part of the Conservatives to dish the rising
S.A. Radical-party.
At one o’clock the other morning, just as
the tired politicians were thinking ot adjourning,
the big clock at Parliament House, Sydney,
struck 13. This is a solemn and dreadful portent.
It obviously means that it will be 13 years before
George Reid gets a show to be Premier of N.S.W.,
and when he does get a show he will be all at
sixes and sevens.
Theodore Unmack, Postmaster-General and
Minister for Railways (Q.), is to be credited with
possession of the courage of independent opinions.
He voted as a solitary Crown Minister against
the grant of £2OOO to Mrs. Macrossan, and the
.£2OOO to Mrs. Jordan. He voted with a small
minority of three or four on each occasion as a
protest on principle against improper grants
proposed and carried as a manifestation of a sen-
timentalism which won’t work all round.
A pleasant comment from the Canterbury
Times (M.L.)
The groat scheme by which Australians were to abandon
local jealousies, parish politics, intercolonial custom-
houses, and all the other objectionable things which the
world sees in Australia, has disappeared. The effort was
too great for enervated Australia. The Australians prefer
the fool’s paradise of borrowed money, with its scrambles
and its sham-patriotism and demoralisation. They have
no stomach for anything higher. Let them grovel!
In supporting the Government Railways
(Contracts) Amendment Bill, the second reading
of which was carried by 85 to 5, Mr. Carruthers,
M.P. (N.S.W.), said he would like to see the
principle which it embodied extended to appoint-
ments, “so that Australians might come first.’
Mr. Carruthers never was so patriotic when his
voice was potent in Government. It was chiefly
though not nominally for affirming too freely the
principle which he commends that a penniless
pressman was sent to gaol last year.
G.,H. Reid, criticising the composition of
the N.S.W. Protectionist Ministry, spoke of it as
“a Ministry of proselytes and not of apostles.”
None of the old warriors who had for so many
years fought for Protection were, he remarked,
included in the Government. “ They had been
crowded out by the proselytes.” And that was
what stung Reid. He remembered sp many of
the Government as brother Freetraders of his,
and yet he could not point to a single convert to
Protection on the Freetrade benches.
The members of S.A. Legislative Council
seem quite heedless of the approaching wrath of
the Democratic party. For three years the Assem-
bly carried by large majorities a Bill for municipal
rating on unimproved values, and the Council
threw it out each time. This year, for the fourth
time, the measure was brought forward, and,
owing (o the Labour-party, there was a majority.
The old fossils bided their time, however, and
when three of its supporters were absent somebody
moved the Chairman out, of the chair, and the
Bill was again wrecked.

An argument for land nationalisation from'
a current issue of Lloyd’s Newspaper (London):
Hunts and Free Education.— John Bell, labourer, was
charged at Newcastle-on-Tyne, on Monday, with having
broken into a shop and stolen clothing to the value of £5.
Daring the case prisoner’s wife complained that her rent
had been raised. Police-superintendent Moss said since
the passing of the Free Education Bill rents had been in-
creased. Alderman Hamond ; Nonsense—is that so ?
Superintendent Moss: Yes: and the thing prevails all
over the place. In reply to Alderman Hamond, prisoner’s
wife said their children went to a Board school. Aider-
man Hamond: And the landlord charges you the fee you
used to pay for the children's education? Very nice,
indeed. Superintendent Moss: It is going on over the
whole district. Alderman Hamond: It is most extra-
ordinary. If the landlords had paid the school fees I
could have understood it. It is most iniquitous, and this
is legislation ! It is grandmotherly legislation. The
prisoner was remanded.
After this, should the State own the land ?

During the period 1867 to 1886 the
number of divorces granted in the U.S. numbered
328,710. There are no less than 42 valid causes for
divorce, one of which, “rash and ungovernablo
temper,” obtains only in Florida. It is, however,
as good as any o'ther reason. The chief real
ground for divorce is, after all, “I do not like
you, Dr. Fell.” The law does not recognise this,
so people swear to all sorts of fairy tales, in order
to comply with the code’s exactions.

He —“Wont you (kiss) give me (kiss, kiss)
just one (kiss, kiss, kiss) kiss, my (kiss, kiss, kiss,
kiss) own?” She —“Yes (kiss, kiss, kiss, kiss,
kiss) darling, just (kiss, kiss, kiss, kiss, kiss, kiss)
one.”—Ex.
A. Glasgow firm is building two Atlantic
liners for the Cunard Cos., each of which will be
GOO feet long, or only eighty feet less than the
Great Eastern, while their indicated horse-power
will be three times as great as that vessel’s.
These will be the largest sea-going passenger boats
in existence.
Night and Day, the organ of philanthro-
pist Barnardo, of London, sends us an urgent
appeal for subscriptions, and requests the helper
to state, when despatching the cash, whether his
—or her —designation is “Mr., Miss, Rev., Mr.,
or Esq.” We hereby inform Barnardo that we
are distinctly “Esq.” Also, we notice there is a
dog for sale at the office, that beast having been
donated to the good cause.
The real, unvarnished facts respecting the
faulty Baldwin engines last imported by the
N.S.W. Commissioners are stated to be as
follows. The engines are unwieldy, and of too
heavy a calibre for the rails, and, notwithstanding
semi-official explanations in the press, are im-
practicable for the swift-running express service.
The order sent was for “ twelve of the type used
on the Baltimore and Ohio railway, but lighter.”
It was the ambiguity of the order, and the absence
of a proper specification, that caused the “muss.”
The order was sent during the absence from the
country of Mr. Thow, who has an invincible
hatred of everything American, and there is sure
to be a nasty complication of engineering talent
when he returns.
The Y. M. C. A. saints have decided to hold a
week of devotion, of which somebody has sent us
the bill: —
1. Preparation for the week of prayer by public
or private meetings ; 2, humiliation and cleansing;
3, the inner life of the associations ; 4, outward activity ;
5, prayer for young men of different social conditions ; 6,
prayer for the committees of the associations; and 7,
prayer for associations suffering from weakness, exposed
to temptation, or isolated, for those in heathen countries,
and for others.
We love these old, unctuous, cant phrases. We
love the humiliation and the cleansing, and the
“ inner life of the associations,” and all the rest of
i the wheezes. There is a pious oiliness about them
which we would miss dreadfully if it were
abolished. We like to hear of the “ outpourings ”
and the “sanctification” and the “blessings”
, and the collection, and the rest of the 700
words or so which make up the entire pulpit
vocabulary of the period. The modern preacher
never talks of cleaning— he always says
“cleansing.'’ He never gets a thing—
it has always been vouchsafed to him; and he
meditates instead of thinking, and goes in heavily
for “humiliation.” But all the same the
Y.M.O.A. doesn’t mean anything. For if it did,
instead of praying for “outward activity” or
things like that it would get down to facts, and
ask to be delivered from hypocrites and snuffle-
busters ; it would pray that the choir-girl and the
.office cash might not go astray so frequently; and
that the old church member might not miscon-
duct himself so often by pursuing small girls.
But no Y.M.O.A. ever does pray for these things;
it keeps on in the old groove asking for “ humilia-
tion ” and “inner life” and ’ “ outward activity,”
and similar muddy old gags all the time.
An all-round swindle is frequently
squared.
Dear Bulletin, —Poor old John Outram
Wascoe—nephew of the distinguished General
Outram—who kept the “Pilgrim’s Inn” on the
Blue Mountains 25 years ago, and who afterwards
obtained a billet in the N.S.W. Government
stores, through the influence of the late Sir
James Martin, came to Wattle Plat two years ago
to be, as he said, “buried alongside of his second
wife,’’ in the R.C. burying-ground. After all, the
dead man’s wishes were not respected, for he was
laid to rest by his friends and heirs in the C.E.
grounds.—Yours truly, S.E.
A certain Queensland Crown Minister was
standing unsteadily in the lobby the other even-
ing when his chief, Sir Samuel, came along. “Do
you know, Mr. , that your estimates come on
this evening?” “Yes, Sir Samuel, I will be
there.” “ Well, had you not better get in then?”
“ When the time comes I will be just as ready as
you are ; and if you think I will not, then accept
my resignation as a Minister of the Crown at
once ?” This flabbergasted Griffith, and later
on in the evening the—hie—Minister put his
“estimates ” through in record time.
The Yictorian rifle-clubs have decided, by
a large majority, not to enforce the wearing of
uniforms, on the ground that such a rule would
cause a large falling off in membership. A gleam
of sunshine breaks through the dark cloud of
militarism which has long been gathering over
the land of “Lay’em out.” The amateur dash-
ing militaire appears to be satisfied with a license
to carry a rifle, and doesn’t pant to doll himself
up in the trappings of war for the sole purpose of
mashing poor young Mary Ann. Meanwhile the
blessed Majah General groans over the indignity
of having to inspect a lot of fellahs who look like
beastly civilians, bai jove ! and he gets up in the
middle of the night to snort at the monstrous
supposition that any man can defend his native
shores in a sac suit. It is ridiculous. The
Bulletin thinks that every adult Australian
should pass his days in tin armour, waiting for
the far-off foe, whilst the women and children
develope the resources of the country and vote
a yearly increase on the Commandant’s salary.
An “ up-country visitor” to Melbourne
complains that the police authorities have
neglected to adopt a thief-catching invention
which he brought under their notice three years
ago. The idea of this bucolic genius is distinctly
Jin de siecle and to the point. He proposes that a
number of detectives, disguised as unsuspecting
graziers, or artless maidens from the bush (the
average D. being constructed on graceful, feminine
lines, seemingly) shall take up commanding posi-
tions on the Block, or scatter themselves among
crowds at agricultural shows, &c., armed" with
aggressive outside pockets calculated to attract
the malefaeting eye. Said pockets are to be
bordered round with strong elastic bands
and fish-hooks, so that the insinuating paw
of the larcenist will remain firmly secured to
the pocket until his yells of anguish arouse the
active and intelligent copper from his normal con-
dition of stupor, whereupon he will turn round
and arrest his bleeding hanger-on in the Queen's
name. This up-country person’s proposal strikes
one as likely to fill a long-felt want, and why
Commissioner Chomley doesn’t dress up a detach-
ment of blue-chinned myrmidons in skirts and
parasols, and set them angling for pickpockets in
public places, is another of those police mysteries
which time alone can unravel. The inventor’s
real name ought to be Hookey Walker.
An old Scotchman is said to have risen in
prayer-meeting one night and, apropos of nothing,
delivered himself of the following remarks ;
“There are three things I never could quite
understand. First, why boys will throw sticks at
green apples when, if they waited, the apples
would ripen and drop off; secondly, I never
could quite comprehend why men go to war to
kill each other when, if they remained quietly at
home they would die a natural death in good
time; thirdly, and most important of all, Ido
not see why the men chase around after the
women, since, if they sat still, the women would
run round after them.” How true that death
comes to all men, to some so prematurely, who
neglect the warning symptoms of approaching
disease, such as a tired aching feeling in the
morning, headaches and nervousness, sour taste
in the mouth, &c., all these are signs that the
physical body is deranged and that if treated
lightly, serious danger is to be apprehended. W.
Swanson, Esq., 70 King William-street, Fitzroy,
Melbourne, says : About three years ago I was
seized all of a sudden with great lassitude, dizzi-
ness and extreme nervousness so great that I was
unable to rally after any effort. My digestive
organs were so impaired and my liver so con-
gested that I was scarcely able to eat any solid
food, and gradually became so weak that I could
barely drag myself along. After using a number
of doctors’ medicines and advertised remedies to
no purpose, I started on a course of Clements
Tonic which brought me groat relief, especially
when using Dr. Fletcher’s Pills as well. Then I
gained weight and strength quickly. I took
altogether 12 bottles of Clements Tonic and four
boxes of Fletcher’s Pills, and my health and vigor
increased under their healthful stimulus.” Miss
Amelia Mensh, 471 King-street, Melbourne,
writes: “I have suffered most acutely from
neuralgia, so muck that my face often swelled
great and caused me much agony. I can truly
say that Clements Tonic gave me prompt and
very great relief.” Head-office and laboratories,
212 A’Beckett street, Melbourne.

• Newcastle, Wednesday.,—A diver named
Macartney, in the employmetft of the Har-
borsand Rivers Department, while at work
in the harbor yesterday under a pontoon near
the pilot boatshed, saw a large sea monster,
fully 30ft. long, with a dog-shaped head and
large eyes, approaching him. Aiacartney.
becoming alarmed, gave the signal to haul
up, and on reaching the. surface fainted from
fright./

MINISTERIAL MODESTY.
A Ryde deputation which waited on the
Postmaster-General yesterday made to that
gentleman the usual suggestion that he
should visit the district himself and be
satisfied of the righteousness of their
demands. They promised that if he did so
t}o would be received with a brass band, as
Mr. Bruce Smith was. Mr. Kidd promptly .
forbade any such demonstration, and subse- -
quently made it clear that if he visited the
district it would only be on condition that he
was not overloaded with any kind of pomp. •
“ I want to come,” he said, “ on a matter of
business, in the quietest way passible. Igo
there for information, not for display. I
don't want any demonstration—l want to go
quietly, find out what you want and get back
as soon as possible. My hands are full
enough of the duties of this office, without
wasting time in any ceremony "

The expected addition to the Hopetoun

# # #
A sinecural servant of the N.S.W. Govern-
ment figured as the slave of sleep at last Mel-
bourne Cup. Shortly after the first race he
wedged himself into a corner of the stand, and,
placing his large and tender hand upon his un-
troubled brow, fell sound asleep. The mad excite-
ment of the Cup rose like a tempest, raged,
and died away. But the unconscious official
functionary heard it not, nor did he stir until the
pangs of hunger warned him that dinner time was
drawing nigh.
#. * *
Thus the “funny ” man of Sydney Echo :
“Dismal Jimmy” has begun again!
How anyone connected with the “establishment’ }
dare refer in such flippant terms to “ Mr. James’ 5
resumption of his arduous duties at the S.M.
Herald office is unexplainable, even on the
grounds of “ wit without coarseness.” Notwith-
standing all the latitude allowed to the Court
fool in the olden times, that privileged functionary
would have got a week’s notice for whacking the
King over the head with a bladder. Perhaps,’
after all, the Echo driveller meant somebody else.
# # *
Everyone knows what a solemn lot the
S.M. Herald authorities are. Well, the other
day, after prayer and lemonade at the “ welcome
home ” to “ Mister James,” the head of the house
of Fairfax, at Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall, a rascal of
a compositor stood up and calmly sang a comic
song. And what do you think it was all about ?
It was about the girl whom he married and who
presented him with twins. What a lark that
song must have been ! It must have been like going
into the chief fane of Buddha with your boots on
and dancing a sand-jig.
* * *
A crank who is occasionally sent to the
lunatic asylum is kept in the Civil Service of an
Australian province through influence. To narrate
the origin of the influence is to relate the story of
a man’s undying love. Thirty years ago the
crank’s sister was deeply loved by a young man
who has since risen to fame. His suit was re-
jected, and in his disappointment he voyaged
abroad, and of course married the first pretty girl
he met. But his first love endureth for ever, and
thus it comes that the astute and inflexible poli-
tician champions the cranky civil servant, who is
privileged to tell his department superior, in the
presence of the whole office, to “go to the devil.”
# * #
After reading the N.S.W. divorce-reports
for the last week or two and hearing about some
Sydney divorces concerning which the papers can
say little, we conclude that I-o-d-g-e-r spells
adultery.
# # •
The joke of the year is the Sydney Daily
Telegraph’s account, in Saturday-week’s issue,
of a certain Arcadian marriage which, it says,
took place under a tree in Middle Harbour. The
report needs to be read slowly and carefully and
not out loud —if any ladies who understand Eng-
lish happen to be around.
# # *
The (now impending) failure of a Sydney
land-dealing company will presently inflict great
misery on many hundreds of small buyers, who,
as they bought land already mortgaged by the
vendors, will be unable to get their deeds. A man
we know submitted to Treasurer M’Millan, in
1889, a memo, of the doings of the principals of
the institution referred t -1 , and suggested a draft
of a clause. Had the hint been acted upon, the
fellow who will presently be execrated by a legion
of poor people as a robber, could only have
defrauded a few persons.
# # #
An apparently bad case of plagiarism is
now exciting attention. It is alleged that an
article sent in to a certain Sydney weekly by a
contributor was rejected but was subsequently
utilised by a lady connected with the journal,
who sent it as her own to England, where it
appeared. When the paper containing it reached
Australia trouble began.
« * a
When The Bulletin starts a daily paper
in Sydney, it will post its reporter on the steps of
the Divorce-court at daybreak, awaiting the open-
ing of the door. As things stand at present,
“select” cases are often heard as early as a
quarter-past nine.
At a recent Australian wedding where the
Governor was present, the p:octodings were
nearly blocked in the final act by the non-arrival
of the best man. This position was to be filled
by a small sized “Majah’’ who had incautiously
attended a party on the previous evening and
devoted the next day to solid repose. As the time
of 11ie ceremony drew near, and the Majah was
nowhere visible on the edge of the horizon, some
one suggested that the post be filled by the
Governor’s aide-de-camp, but just as that im-
portant person had fixed in position and straight-
ened his pants at the knees, the “Majah” came
tearing down the aisle wearing the costume ho
had slept in that day, and an expression of in-
dignation and undigested champagne and unsatis-
fied thirst for human blood. Chaos was only
narrowly averted by skilled diplomatists, and
when it was all over the party started for the
wedding feast and the bridegroom carved the cake
with his sword.
# # #
A well-known Australian Judge, whose
lips are always silently adding up, subtracting,
and finding the remainder, as ho threads his way
through the busy mart for the purpose of floating
a new loan —with which to pay the interest on the
previous one—stuck up a wealthy resident of a
marine suburb, ouo Sunday morning, and begged
for £5O. Strangely enough, the retired capitalist
knew not that his Honor was the gaunt genius of
hopeless impecuniosity and famine, and let him
have it. Mentioning the transaction to the local
butcher he was horrified to realise that the debt
was about as good as a Liberian debenture, A
month after the Judge again came, and with pro-
fuse apologies attempted to tap his simplicity for
another £25. The contumelious language used by
the victim of misplaced confidence was perfectly
shocking, and but for his Honor’s hasty departure
it would have developed into violence.

Games of all kinds are strictly forbidden
within the walls of Darlinghurst Prison, the
reasonable theory being that not only would such
indulgences help to deprive prison of its proper
terrors, but that they would lead to gambling,
“battle, murder, and sudden death.” Neverthe-
less, the prohibition is regularly evaded, in most
instances with the connivance of the warders. In
No. 4 yard, which contains the cream of the
burgling profession, the quantity of tobacco staked
on eacli racing-event would astonish the Comp-
troller. The Chosen people here, as outside, run
their totes, consultations, and sweeps, and the
accuracy of their knowledge with respect to
fixtures, favourites, and the state of the
betting-market would be marvellous if their
envied privileges were not known to be so
utterly utter. Draughts arc successfully nego-
tiated with black and white buttons on a slate
marked off into 01 squaws, and chess is pflayed
with even greater facility, inasmuch as_ no
“pieces” are required; letters denoting king,
queen, bishop, knight, and castle, strokes and
circles the prawns, whilst moves areeffected simply
enough by rubbing out one letter and substituting
another. This is essentially the diversion of the
sedate lifer and the suave defaulter. More than
one prisoner has done his seven days’ cells for
manufacturing cards.

* # *
Photographed appearances are deceiving.
A certain noble Britisher, not peer enough to
catch tin Australian heiress, and consequently too
blue-blooded to see anything jn_4-lio doosid colo-
nials, fell in love with a countrywoman on seeing
her photograph in the album of a fellow-exile, who
was the lady’s brother. He confessed his pflight
to the brother, who good-naturedly arranged that
the p)arties should correspond. All went well.
The lovers, between whom the whole earth inter-
posed itself, cooed to each other on paper and
fixed the happy day. The expectant bridegroom
paid her passage out, but, all, sad fate ! his prize
appeared in the flesh, little better than a museum
specimen with red, tow hair and a devilish squint.
Result: Refused to marry her, and had to piawn
his immortal soul so as to get the cash to pro-
pitiate the incensed brother and the outraged
maiden.

She was a female witness, and when the
court asked her age she returned it as twenty-five.
The next witness announced himself as the son of
the foregoing, and gave his age as twenty-seven.

Agnes Conneary, a young woman employed
in the Federal Coffee Palace (Melbourne), has be-
come a lunatic through attending Booth's meet-
ings. When she was run in she made a statement
to the effect that Booth was the direct successor
of Christ. This item, when taken alone, doesn’t
amount to much, for the Gened is obviously of the
same opinion himself, but possibly there was other
evidence.

# # #
History repeats itself—upside down. By
the last mail there came news from France that a
sportsman had shot a baby in mistake for a hare.
At Hill End, N.S.W., last week a boy shot his
father dead in mistake for a dog.
# # #
There is quite a matrimonial epidemic
amongst Australians in London, writes a corres-
pondent. The latest victim is Mr. Baynes Dick-
son, of South Yarra, who has buckled himself to
the widow of a Cjlonel Borthwick. The lady has
already buried two husbands, and evidently (as
the Irish say) has kept the needle which stitched
their shrouds.
# # *
In Western Australia, according to the
recent census, there arc five unmarried men to
every single woman above the age of 21. On the
basis of the whole of the population of that pro-
vince there are three males to every two females.
In the Northern districts the males greatly pre-
dominate, the proportion of females to males
being a little over 9 per cent. The youngest wife
in Western Australia is under 14 years.

ciation, unfortunately allowed a letter evidencing
his corrupt collusion with a marker to fall into
the hands of an official for whom it was not
intended.

* # #
A Sydney evening paper publishes a recipe
for removing bloodstains. That paper is an ass.
If people get to know how to remove bloodstains
then what is to become of the unfathomable detec-
tive in its Saturday tale—the sleuth-hound who
goes around with a dark lantern at midnight and
finds the red blotch on the floor of the ruined
house, by which he instantly spots the duke who
assassinated the girl with the soft brown eye and
threw her remains into the tank? Then how is
the duke to take Rough on Rats and die in agony
on the mat at the foot of the stairs, after writing
a confession which is afterwards discovered hidden
in a box, in a hole under a withered tree with one
bough standing up and pointing to the moon? And
after that, ho v is the girl with the soft brown eye
to come to life again and forgive him, and spend
all the rest of her life in works of benevolence;
and lib'w is the spectre to come and sit on the
bloodstain in the dark, and chatter, and shake its
bones at the stars ? And if these things are
abolished, how is the evening paper to have a tale
of any kind?
* # *
The man who was responsible for putting
barbed-wire strands over the wooden picket-fence
of St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney,—whether he
wears a biretta or a plug-hat—ought to be
indicted as a public nuisance. No action is more
natural than for a person pausing to view the
noble architecture of the building to place his
hand on the fence, but if anyone does so, now, he
risks laceration. In Victoria, so much pain has
been inflicted upon the lower animals by barbed-
wire that its employment is now regulated.

Miss Creo Stanley and her sister-delegate
to N.S.W. Trades and Labour Council are both
opponents of the Womanhood Suffrage League.

Mr. Fletcher Watson has been elected
President of the “ Academy of Arts of Australia,”
the newly-organised rival of the N.S.W. Art
Society. Let us hope Mr. Watson won’t call his
academy the “Royal ” Academy, and make into
R.A.’s and A.R.A.’s a lot of people who paint
pictures photographically.

anyone getting married by special license before a
R.C. priest had better stump up a good deal more
than a mere fiver, as at that figure the transaction
represents a dead loss to his reverence, who has
A SENTIMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She stood with her face upturned to mine
(Twas a childlike face and rare),
As the day went down and the aftershine
With a glory tinged her hair ;
And a red glow dipped on the far skyline,
As we stood and watched it there.
Dim night on the earth spread wide her pall,
And my hot lips pressed her hair.
As it swept unbound in its rippling fall
To her neck, round, white, and hare ;
And a low wind sang in the branches tall,
“ She is thine for e’er and e’er !”
Sweet hours of the night on swift wings flew,
Ami my soul hurst forth unbound ;
For her lips were red, and her eyes were blue,
And her arms entwined mo round,
And her kiss was balm, and her breath was dew,
And the dim world swooned and drowned.
Close, close to my breast her white cheek pressed
And tier bosom swayed with sighs,
As a faint light waned in the far-off west,
Anil a cloud mass skimmed the skies,
And her head lay hack in my arms at rest,
And I kissed her half-veiled eyes.
Close clasped to my heart I held her fast,
With a fierce, wild love new-born,
And I cried, “ at last!”
. . . . And the wind swept past
With a moan and a cry forlorn,
And a harsh voice mocked, “at last! at last!”
And my love from my arms was torn.
Brisbane.
K.

Professor Morris, to whom Australasia is
chiefly indebted for its blessed Charity Organisa-
tion, got on to the old, old story again at last
week’s conference. His speech was not intended
for the apostles of charity there present, because
they were all delegates (or Lord Hopetoun), and
already knew that God doesn’t love the cheerful
giver unless ho makes enquiries which usually
result in nothing being given. Professor M. was
evidently addressing the ignorant, unorganised
outsiders when ho said : “ They must be careful
not to sap independence, and to remember that
pauperisation was worse than poverty. Out-
door relief should be given with discre-
tion, and constant and continuous doles avoided.”
He didn’t advise large lump sums instead of
“doles,” nor, in fact, did the Professor recom-
mend anything special in the way of charity
except “ a largo central office where the charitable
institutions could have their offices.” And where,
we presume, the associated assisters of the poor
and needy could refer each applicant from
office to office until he came gasping back to
the first office and was told to wait until the
secretary returned from a month’s holiday. Let
us avoid the giving of doles, anyway, because it
comes less expensive to the capitalist, and can t be
worse for Lazarus. Makes him more doleful than
ever, donchersee —
E. P. Wilson, one of the three directors
of the V.D.L. Bank, returned to Hobart with the
last English mail. He was doing the “grand
tour ” when the news of the failure reached him.
The hard knock he has received has altered him
almost beyond recognition.
* # #
At Hobart the case of alleged malpractice
against Dr. Bingham Crowther, of Hobart, and
Mr. Reginald Ritchie has been adjourned sine die,
Crowther not having been heard of since the case
was opened. Ritchie’s bail of ,£9OO has been
released, but that of the runaway “Bing.” will go
to gladden the heart of Treasurer Bird. Crowther
is popularly supposed to be on board the 'Frisco
mail-boat now near her destination. The
absconder has immense influence, and had the
authorities been in a hurry to catch him he could
easily have been arrested at Auckland.
* # #
A S.A. Salvationist has sent £4 to George
Riddoch, a local squatter, as conscience-money.
He says he stole two sheep, value 10s. each, but
the Bible taught that ho must return it “four-
fold,” and he did SO.
* * #
Lewis Cohen, Mayor of Adelaide, and
Governor Kiutore don’t speak, at least so rumour
says. The trouble was all over the naval recep-
tion arrangements. There is much local comment
over the fact that Kintore absented himself from
Cohen's annual banquet, and little Cohen followed
suit by “not being there ” when Kinty’s garden-
party came off.
The V.D.L. Bank buildings will be sold
in December. The Commercial, the Union, and
the Bank of Australasia will all bid.

Insurance Society, Melbourne, stand on the site
of old Johnny Fawkner’s paper, the Pout Phillip
I ATftiOT, which merged into the Argus.

scale ” of suddenly-defuncts does a Sydney Coroner
begin to make enquiries ? Just below that of a
Legidative Councillor ?

A particular birth-notice in an Australian
paper last week has given occasion to members of
a certain religious body to reiterate their belief
that canonical vows cannot be abrogated without
incurring ‘ the divine judgment ” here below.
* # #
One of Sir Benjamin Benjamin’s hospitable
freaks is to send every morning to every guest in
his house a certain confection, one of the young
B. s being the bearer. The pleasant little cere-
mony is never omitted.

Some low character writes in the Edin-
burgh Weekly Scotsman complaining that the
Australian girls are not a success.—
I have seen much of married life, both at home and
abroad in New South Wales, and still I am not married. I
nave been looking for a wife years back, and am no
lurtner forward. The colonial young ladies would be the
last tor me or any man to marry, as they are all very fast-
gomg. Give them dress, drink, and company, and down
tne harbour pic-nicking. The married ladies are the
ALL THE DIFFERENCE.
WIFE: “ John, it makes me so miserable to see you drinking like that.”
HUSBAND: “Nonsensh, my dear; you’re out of spirtsh.”
WIFE (solemnly) : “Ah, John, I wish you were out of spirits.”
worst. The poor husbands toil away under a heat of from
90 to 100 degrees, and the women leave their house to
take care of itself. I know a man who would not have an
Australian wife, and who came homo and got one and
went back. That is just what I would have done if I had
intended going hack. You may get one that Is brought
up in the country that will make a very good wife, hut it
is very hard to come across them.
We know- exactly the sort of wife that complainant
wants. She is a haggard female with a fresh baby
every year. When she goes out she is always
humping one infant and hauling six more, and
she is consumed with anxiety lest the five she has
left at home are eating tin-tacks and trying to
boil the kitten. She has the rheumatism in both
her copious knees through scrubbing the attics
and stairs, and she does all her own washing.
Generally she rises at 5 a.m., and she seems
to be scouring infants with monkey soap right
on till 11 p.m., after which she has all the rest
of the time to herself. When she is dressed
she looks as if her clothes had been shot at her
out of a gun, and her boots are large and loose
and cheap and shapeless. In most cases she dies
of old age at 50, and is glad to do it, for she never
had a rest in all her .toilsome existence before.

The number of ladies, from 18 to .50 years
of age. applying for positions as nurses in Bris-
bane Hospital is now beyond ordinary calculation.
This is chiefly due to the fact that three nurses
from that festive establishment have recently been
married to three doctors.

* # *
One of Sydney D.T.’s correspondents
writes thus on the marriage question :
Many a single woman designated “old maid” walks
alone—not because she could not marry, but because she
is true and faithful to the memory of one.
We know this type of “old maid,” with her time-
worn story of how she could, but she wouldn’t.
The steadfastness with which widows invariably
remain true to the memory of the dead Adolphus
whom they planted in the (sob) spring, casts a
lurid light on the constancy of the spinster to the
memory of the angel whom she never quite
caught.
.# # *
In N.S.W. Full Court (Tyson Divorce
appeal):—
Dr. Sly : “My learned friend attempted to make Tyson
out as all that was pure and noble.”
Sir Julian Salomons: “I have done many things as
counsel, but I have never attempted to do such a thing as
that.”
Which, from the lips of a man whom he had fee’d
handsomely, must have been very pleasing to Mr.
Tyson. And then the Salomons proceeded to do
one of those many things ” which lawyers do as
counsel, thus :
Mr. Justice Innes : “ Marriage or no marriage, there is
no suggestion that she was ever unfaithful (o him.”
Dr. Sly said that the other side admitted that.
Sir Julian Salomons : “We admit it. At any rate, we
have no evidence of it.”
How graceful !

Matthias Larkin, the respectable embezzler
of South Melbouime, is quite unable to say what
he did with his missing cash. He certainly did
not squander it over the prompt settlement of
drapers’ bills. One Melbourne firm has an account
of £lBO or so against the good man.
# # *
One of the engineers discarded when the
present N.S.W. Railway Commissioners came
into office, committed suicide a week or ten days
ago. He never recovered “the sack,” and took
to the flowing bowl. He was a German, and
admittedly one of the ablest men in the Govern-
ment service.
» « 9
Facetious convicts in Darlinghurst prison,
when they want to “sell” a now warder, procure
from tiie boot-shop an oblong piece of solo-
leather, affix to it a tag, easily got from the tin-
department, and drop it near the enemy’s
beat. The face of the victim when he picks up
the fictitious contraband plug—only soogee twist
being allowed—is food for mirth and conversation
in that palace of uneventful routine.
* * #
Rev. Chas. Duppuy, ivho recently died,
was the clergyman who conducted with such per-
fervid zeal the crusade against the teredos erected
in St, Andrew’s Cathedral, Sydney. He didin-
calculable good in educating the Australian public
to a perception of the great truth that a reredos
is not an ibis, nor a dado, but simply a marble
blister, the contemplation of which is conducive
to devotion, just as gazing at a coin in the palm
of your hand helps towards hypnotism. Mr.
Duppuy’s widow and family are 'in destitute cir-
cumstances, and a fund is being raised to send
them to their relatives in England. The Bishop
of Sydney will receive donations.

Ct. M. Bowden, in the Fortnightly
Review for January: “When the late Lord
Ampthill applied to Pone Pius IX. for permission
to form a Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Animals in Rome, the latter replied that ‘it was
a theological error to suppose that man owes any
duty to an animal ’!”
Religion, ” remarks an American writer,
is to most people a good deal like mushrooms, to
be swallowed on faith in an impersonal green-
grocer. If you will notice few dare partake of this
fungus when they are acquainted with the
gatherer. Ihe idea undoubtedly is that no mere
man is smart enough to tell a mushroom from a
toadstool.”
The skipper whoso practice of medicine
consists of treating his crew with emetics for
maladies above the belt, and purgatives for those
below, although a crude one, is more sensible than
that of the many who fly to pills and purgatives
for every ill. But in a relaxing climate like this
the use of a wholesome, bracing preparation like
Marshall’s Tonic Nervine is a far more rational
line of treatment, especially for cases where it is
not deemed necessary to call the doctor in.
M. De Blowitz writes of “Germany,
France, and General European Politics.” His
prediction is that there will be no general war just
yet. The balance of parties, the dread of im-
perialism, must keep France from taking the
initiative. Italy is too weak, Russia too much
burdened with debt, famine, and Nihilism to
begin. Germany has nothing to gain by it. But
when the Emperor of Austria dies, Germany,
Russia, and Italy will simultaneously reach for
portions of his heterogeneous empire, and the vast
military establishments which now oppress Europe
will accomplish their destiny.
Rudyard Kipling, the impudent Indian
storyteller who landed in Australia on a Monday
and left the following Thursday, and who in
the meantime contrived to tell a hew nation that
;t was perfunctory and idiotic and playing at
legislation, is a result of the blind trust placed by
the conservative sections of this community in the
opinions of any commonplace person hailing from
King land. It has been the custom to consider
that the visitor who is able to write stories is per-
fectly at homo on legislation and metallurgy
much in the same way as a blacksmith would be
supposed to have an illicit intimacy with
astronomy. If Gladstone should come here and
remark that our Governmental administration was
corruption, the fact of the speaker being Gladstone
would gi ve sufficient colour to render investigation
advisable. But we would not take Gladstone’s
opinions on the idiotic heroism of the British
soldier and the effect of Indian stories on the
morality of the world any more than we would
trust Kipling to describe us. There is absolutely
but one thing in this country whose efficacy as a
curative all Australians are able to endorse. The
Bethel Electric Medical Baths Co., of Lonsdale-
street, Melbourne, have during the last six months
performed wonderful cures of nervous, digestive,
and circulatory diseases, and any man or woman
in the Australia® suffering from debility, gout,
and blood diseases, and all derangements of vital
functions can, by using Bethel’s patents, compass
a certain cure at a moderate cost. There is but
one Electric Curative method in Australia, and so
all sufferers wishing relief must journey to Lons-
dale-street, Melbourne, and the writer of this is
thafeure’s'prophet.*
TO THE PROFESSOR.
The clouds are lifting, and at last
We see our duty clearly :
The folks we pitied in the past
We’ll pity still, sinc;rely :
But ne’er assist, with ready hand,
A “ case” we do not “ understand.’*
When any hungry-looking chap
Begs hard for an oblation.
We can discharge, without a “ rap,”
Our Christian obligation,
And send him empty from our door
To find a reason why he’s poor.
No mother of a moaning child
(Pretending to be sickly)
Shall bore us with petition mild,
We’ll send her (lying quickly,
’Twould shocking he if, by our aid,
That woman were a pauper made.
We’ll hid the gents, and ladies, too,
Who seem in want of feeding,
To get a guarantee from you
That money they are needing ;
And if their stories you should doubt,
We hope and trust you’ll chuck ’em out
We did not know our duty when
With baccy we were spoiling,
From time to time, some aged men,
Who haven’t strength for toiling ;
We fear their weak, immortal souls
Were ruined by those “constant doles.”
The penny, or the silver “sprat,”
Is not a seed of kindness,
When scattered in the battered hat
Of one whose case is blindness ;
We will not heed his bitter cry
For fear it may he all my eye.
Our conscience-we will cover up,
No starving wretch shall prick it—
We’ll give him neither bite nor sup
Unless he bring a ticket
From Goldstein’s office or from Jones-
“ Regale the hearer with some bones.”
Professor Morris, we will pray
For strength to keep from giving
Demoralising coin away
To any beggar living,
The doles we used to waste on tramps
You'll get, for circulars and stamps,
And offices, and clerks galore,
And secretaries polished,
AVho’ll “ look into the matter more,”
Till poverty’s abolished,
And all the folks we haven’t fed
Are doing well at being dead.
"Wild Patsy.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Och ! jump around, boys, here’s the hilly a-hoiiing,
Hie sun will be up in a moment, I guess ;
Look smart now, the curry will soon he a-spoiling,
If it s cold, faith! you’ve only yourselves for to bless.”
Joe and I, with a yawn, jump up out of our “blueys”—
But Alec, snores on in his hunk for a while—
‘ Wild Patsy ” gives vent to the wildest of coo-eys,
Just to “let off the steam,” as he says with a smile.
There were six of us camping on Kangaroo Creek—
No, stop ! there were seven, including the boss—
We had been there a day or two over a week,
But the creek we had not yet been able to cross.
We had surveyed two blocks for old Joshua Hyde,
The boundaries all had been carefully “run,”
But we wished to “ connect” to the opposite side,
’Twas dangerous work, hut it had to he done.
We finished the breakfast old Pat had prepared,
(Well, as for the curry, we finished the lot),
Made straight for the-creek, for the boss had declared,
He d tackle the water—a “ hanker ”or not.
When arrived at the hank, it was just as we thought,
The waters were roaring and tearing apace ;
While every current fresh obstacles brought—
A whirling of debris—a caution to face.
For a moment or two we stood still on the brink,
No eagerness any displayed for the trip,
Till Patsy volunteer’d with a hearty, “ 1 think,
I’ll try it, and chance it-hcre goes for a strip.”
He was stripp’d in a moment, and taking the chain
He started those turbulent rapids to breast,
With a joking, “ Perhaps I shan’t see you again ”
Oh ! “ many’s the truth that is uttered in jest!”
Then in silence we cazed on this gallant display
Of presence of mind in a ciitical case,
While he battl’d and struggled and fought on his way
As the waters rushed on in a hurricane race.
And now he is close to the opposite land—
The current seems less on the furthermost side—
When, without any warning, he throws up his hand,
“God help me !” his cry—and he sinks ’neath the tide
We did what we could—hut ah ! never again,
Would Patsy’s gay laugh echo in with the rest—
No more would be ready in trouble or pain
To turn both aside with a smile or a jest.
So, sadly we placed him to rest ’neath the shade
Of a willow that wept in its silence alone :
On a cross of red cedar, these letters we made—
“ Died bravely in harness-Wild Patsy Malone.”

“ H.J.K.” : Don’t remember receiving anything
In your handwriting—“ A.C.F.” (Lane C.): Is it abso-
lutely original ?,...“ The Larrikin”: We don t give
“ turns” to gentlemen who have a plurality of wives....
“Jim Byrnes”: Thanks. We are obliged for your
criticism “P.H.” (York): Purely local. Send us
something of all-round interest.. “Jarrobp”; If you
will read an article entitled “Things will last my time,
and published in Tnr. Bulletin of 31st May, 1890, you
will find all your arguments, and a good many more. It
is unkind to dish up cur own brains and send them to
us, though it still affords us a certain amount of pleasure
to see the old screed turning up again.... “ Kangaroo :
We are very pious, and your poetry is horribly
heterodox “ Pontifex Maximus ”: We got your poem,
for which the W.P.B. yawned, and not in vain....
“ Yendys” : Good verse in a way, but spoilt by too much
cant-democratic phrase —allusions to the “ tyrant’s heel,
and old sayings like that. Moreover, to run in the
tyrant’s heel apropos of Wales is absurd ; you can’t make
a tyrant out of baldness, baccarat-counlers, and jelly.. .
“D. O’R.” You must be the great original Jabberwock,
judging by your verses....“ Bertie Cecil” (Yass); A
rather pretty love-song, but, excepting works of absolute
genius, we don’t care over-much about love-songs. If
you ever offered cold tea to an alligator you will under-
stand what we mean “Thiggum Thu”: We have
always said 50.... “ A Haas ” : You should be arrested on
suspicion for writing a thing like this :
“ I rise to go, I leave my seat, it is the 14th day of May,
Instead of travelling towards my home I am going the
other way.”
If you do things like that often you will be sentenced some
day to be chained up and read to death by wild poets....
“To Hoa ArohaTawhi” : Thanks “Chain Lightning :
Rhymeless and rhythmless. Must decline....“ A Con-
stant Reader”: We have a score of limes during our

justice... “ Sans Souci ”: Thanks for letter....“ Childe
Harold”: On 28th November, 1889, we believe....“ Gee-
bun"-” (Lismore) : If you will write to the Insurance and |

Temp.”: Too pious to be honest “A.M.J.”: We dint

“ modestlv ”... “ Bris ” : Some of the most pleasant
