SPORTING NOTIONS.
Mullagh, the celebrated aboriginal
cricketer, spoke English, as we have said, without the
slightest trace of the “ blacks’” accent. On one occasion
when the aboriijinal team played a match on the old
Albert Ground, Sydney, between the innings the darkies
were much lionized. T. C. Jamison, a well-known Sydney
character, a locker or something in the Customs, went up
to Mullagh and addressed him in the supposed native
lingo. “How you bin likit gricket, Mullagh?” said he ;
“you likum blay longa white fellow?” “I beg your
pardon, sir,” quoth Johnny, “I don’t understand what
you say ; I speak English.”
f * *
Joe Thompson, writes our correspondent, must
have won an enormous sum of money over the
Caisarewitch. He made his book for Alec. Taylor’s stable
—it is said for Harfieur, but no doubt the wily Joseph
knew all about Ragimunde. There is some mystery about
the running of this horse, whicli only a fortnight ago
could only beat a commoner like Sheldrake at New-
market after a dead heat. In the big handicap he
won all the way. Such a reversal of form would
certainly lead to enquiries if the horse belonged to any
one but a swell like the Duke of Beaufort. In addition
to his direct winnings Joe must have cleared the whole of
his enormous double-event book. Joe is fairly at the top
of the tree as a fielder. He is known now at the clubs as
“the Leviathan,”of which he is very proud.
# * *
O’Connor and Hanlan seem to have beaten
Dutch, of the Parramatta, and Stephenson, of Maoriland,
very badly in the three mile-sculling race on Fraser
River, British Columbia. There’s a good deal in the
‘ my foot is on my native heath ” feeling.
* * m
The usual prosecution of unregistered book-
makers attending Melbourne Cup eventuated at the
local Court the other day when about a dozen sweep and
game men were fined or sent to gaol. The prosecution in
the case of one Hyands was based on the fact that
defendant had been given into the custody of the police
by “ some man who had betted on a card game and lost.”
Nqw, The Bulletin would like to know what would have
happened if Hyands had lost the bet and given the “ some
man ” in charge for playing an unlawful game ?
• # *
That mysterious “ Englishman named Wells,”
who is perpetually winning fortunes at Monte Carlo looks,
at this distance, uncommonly like a myth, with a
“system” of bringing “visitors flocking from all parts to
the tables.” Probably the cablecrammer has muddled up
the real facts of the case. Some of our old nobility are
having little runs of luck, perhaps, and the croupiers
utter maledictions on “the Ingleesh swells.” Where-
upon, an addle-pated British newspaper correspondent
wires, “Englishman Wells Twenty-'thousand” (meaning
francs), the London dailies “expand” their intelligence
in tlie usual way, and the Australian cablecrammer lays
hold of it and adds the romantic supposition that Wells
started operations with a £5 note.
* * *
Melbourne Cup of ’9l is now ancient history,
but in the agony column of the Victorian papers there
still appears the customary pathetic notice praying that
the individual wearing a tall hat who held the £2
sweep will please write at once to X.YZ. and oblige.
This sort of advt. appears after every Cup, but nothing
is ever gained thereby save by the newspapers. Any-
body who puts up money in a sween with a party in a
bell-topper and glaring check raiment should get that
stakeholder down and stand on him till the race is over, or
else he should padlock him up to the fence. Or he might
lock him up in a room somewhere, and confiscate = his
clothes as security, and then leave the naked stakeholder
to sit on the money till settling time. And now that we
come to think of it, bookmakers should also be naked—
their clothes being deposited with the committee-and
they should bet through a pigeon-hole. Then, if they
bolted, the law could get at them and yet not recognise
betting. They would simply be arrested for undue want
of bathing costume.
* # *
Dear Bulletin,—ln correction of a last week’s
correction. Joe Thompson was not on board the City of
Melbourne during her equinoctial voyage of ’76 ; he re-
mained behind, with his brother “ books,” for the Sydney
Turf Club meeting, held one week after the night
the steamship sailed. Joe, however (an old shell-
back, by the way), was first man on board on our arrival
in smooth water. I was on board, and so was Mrs. Jack
Thompson and baby—number one, I believe. I still
remember the big tears welling up out of the
warm-hearted, burly “ booky’s ” eyes as he (Jack) folded
mother and child in one embrace. I have reason
to remember the circumstances of- the trip, for
returning to Sydney perforce, I waited to go in the
Alexandra, s.s., and backed Planet and Hotspur for
Hurdle and Steeple, at the S.T.C Meeting, at 20 to 1.
Corrigan’s mount won by a head from Glenister's grey
horse, named Grey something or other, and Hotspur,
with over 12 stone on his back, starting at 6 to 4, was
odds on when Nonsense, the late Mr. Bowes’ horse, with
the late George Mason up, I think, fell. It was’ odds
then on Hotspur, and I could have stood to win a big sum
one way and a fair sura the other for a brief space, but
there was nothing to make running, and Harding’s
Fearnaught, taking the fences at leisure, won easily from
the weight-crushed Hotspur. I might also tell you how
I paid a well-known Victorian steeplechaser 6s. as my share
of a bag of oysters on the final return trip, and had two
of the “delicious bivalves” for my money.—Yours
Friday.
* # #
Calvinistically-ruled Melbourne beats Sydney
at lawn-tennis. The chief reason is that Melbourne plays
lawn-tennis halt Saturday and all day Sunday. Sydney
never plays on Sunday. Thus is piety rewarded.
* * *
About the most pleasant spectacle to be seen
on Sydney cricket-playing grounds is a player with the
physique of a professional pug. who wears double bangles
and four rings, while in the field.
* * #
The professional members of Sheffield’s cricket-
team are all excellent warblers. They have been delight-
ing Adelaide friends by their rendition of carols. Abel,
the little Surrey man, leads the crowd.
* # #
Lord Sheffield relies on the crowd wanting to
see Grace to get his money back. Huge posters, about
16 x 14, labelled “ W. G. GRACE ” adorned all the hoard-
ings in Adelaide and suburbs.
* * *
Palo Alto has equalled Maud S.’s mile in 2.8|,
so says the cable. Maud S. made her record on July 30,
1885, when eleven years old, and for six years - has she
held the record. Palo Alto is by Electioneer, and is now
nine years old. Senator Stanford, his owner, said that
for years his stud-groom wanted to call every likely colt
after the name of his estate and breeding-farm, Palo Alto,
but Mr. Stanford insisted on having a say in the matter,
and when he named the horse under notice events now
show that he picked the correct animal to perpetuate the
name of Palo Alto in sporting circles. In 1887 and 1888
the horse was retired from the track and did stud duty,
but in 1889 he was again brought out, and the following
list of Ifs pci formances in ISS9 arid 1890 will show" that he
was gradually working up to his cabled grea t performance:

Johnel Sullivan got back to America dead
broke. The once mighty bruiser landed on the wharf at
San Francisco a huge, insolvent mass of flesh, and had to
overawe a cabman with his glaring eye ere he could get
himself and bjs luggage shifted to a hotel. He couldn't
even raise the regular fare, hut he paid half of it, and
threatened to wreck the cab when he was asked for the
balance.

Grace’s cricket team drops the Mr. as a distinc-
tion between genii men and players, but gives the former
initials, while the players go without. This will go, too.
* * *
The so-called amateur English cricketer is in-
comparably better paid under the heading ®f expenses
than tlie common pro. In English matches where a
gentleman receives £25 for expenses, the pro. gets a
simple fiver. That’s all the difference between an English
amateur and a professional. The one is a gentleman
handsomely remunerated, the other is a mere man paid
in accordance with the name and its vulgar attributes.
• * *
The only unpaid amateur alive is the Australian.
In the forthcoming matches with the Britishers our rep-
resentatives won’t get a cent.
* * #
Likewise the interprovincial matches are all
played for love and glory. The expenses of tlie team are
paid for the team, but the individual members do not get
a copper as expenses or anything else.
• * *
The average cricketer is often a monstrous baby.
Fancy a six feet high, 35 year old man walking to tjie
wickets kissing his hat and chirping; “This is Mr. Hit-
’em hard, and this is Mr. Hit-’em-hard’s bat.”
* # #
Somebody estimates that in the four days of the
Cup carnival there was exactly one hour and two minutes
of actual racing. Think of that! There were thousands
of columns of newspaper row and rot, stacks of expensive
costumes, hundreds of cablegrams and telegrams, and
oceans of swearing ; there were consultations and
totalisator sweeps innumerable ; every bookie in Australia
had been hoarse for three months with sheer yelling!
every tout had got rheumatism through going out early
to look for the straight griffin ; scores of individuals were
short in their cash through embezzling their boss’s money
to pile on a horse; scores more had arranged to take
somebody else’s wife to see the show, and thus sowed the
seeds of a divorce case ; half the nation was drunk ; about
five Parliaments were adjourned ; every small boy in this
continent was demoralised ; there were special trains and
special steamers ; there were miles of dreary preaching
about the horrors of gambling ; there was much nobbling
and lying and laying of huge wagers ; sundry persons won
large sums and started to drink themselves right to the
red-hot brass gates of Sheol, and all Australia was turned
upside down. And all for one hour and two minutes fun,
at the rate of fifteen minutes per day ! Great Caesar!

Bigcrowd at Sydney Olympic on Saturday night
when no less than three tights “to a climax” were
brought off, the principal being between Winters and

rounds, when the darkey curled up and cried a go. A

silencing his man in some fifty seconds.

The Austral wheel race, two miles, which came
off on Melbourne Cricket Ground on Saturday, is a race
worth winning, as it carries with it a cash prize of £2OO.
Forty-two started, and H. H. Lambton was much fancied,
but the winner turned up in A. Turner, who had 240
yards start.

Eden, Twofold Bay, holds a regatta on Decem-
ber 28, the principal fixture being an All-Comers Handi-
cap Race, with Ist, 2nd and 3rd prizes of £4O, £l5 and
£5. This race should draw some of the metropolitan
scullers, as the Union S.S. Co. will only charge competi-
tors £2 return fare, and carry boats free.
* * *
The meeting between Billy Murphy and Jem
Burge at the Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club on Monday
night ended in a fiasco. For 27 rounds Murphy belted
Burge terribly about the ribs, Burge only relieving the
monotony by now and then standing in tipi middle of the
ring v#ith his jaw buried in his muscular shoulder, whilst
Murphy made ineffectual and savage attempts to “ knock
him out.” Burge did not seem the Burge of old, and
towards the last looked—very disheartened. Murphy’s
hands smashed up badly on the iron man, and as the pair
merely walked round and looked at each other for the
28th, and 29th rounds, the referee called the men down and
declared “no fight.” Murphy did all the fighting and
never received a blow worthy of the name from first to
last, and if only out of humanity to Burge the clubs ought
to now let him drop out of the fighting. Burge has pro-
bably taken more punishment in his career than any six
of the modern fighters put together, and it really seems a
pity to longer smash up scientific hands upon his cast-iron
frame. The “ noble art ” loses much of its manliness and
tends more to brutality when the punishment is all one-
sided, and in the interest of the sport the blubs would do
well to remember this when arranging their fixtures. The
club has now awarded Murphy £4O ; Burge gets nothing.
# * *
The “Demon” in Bathurst Times writes in-
terestingly of a trip to his boyhood’s home at Coombing
Park, Carcoar, N.S.W., where his father, the gallant old
Parson Naylor, of Norfolk Island and elsewhere, lived
many happy years after his labours amongst the convicts
had ceased with the abolition of transportation. Interest-
ing reminiscences are given of the Icely stud, at the Park,
the home of the great sires, Sir Hercules, Gil Bias, and
The Friar. The boxes are still in the same condition as
they were over 40 years ago, the plates with the name of
each horse-occupant being still on the doors. The Icely
estate is now the property of Frank Whitney, of Cobb
and Co., and is devoted exclusively to sheep-breefii ng T t
is one of the loveliest spots in Australasia.

Dear Bulletin,—The population of Oberon
(N.S.W.) is composed principally of one family, and the
name of that family is one that from time immemorial,
especially on the Quid Sod, has been connected with shil-
laleghs, whisky, skittles, lights, and elections—to wit-
well, O'Fl.ynn. A coupleofyears ago two Sydney bookies, re-
splendent’in shiny hats, gaudy coats, and pants of a very
loud and large pattern, visited the Oberon race-meeting
“to take down the mugs.” The first and only bet was
witli one of the members of the O’F. clan named Chry-
sostom. Chrysostom deposited £2 at odds of 3to 2, arid
the rest of the family awaited the result of the race with
some anxiety, knowing that if Chrysostom lost there
would be the “divil to pay.” The race was run—Chrys.
lost. Tlie solitary policeman was away having a drink.
. . . That night those two bookmakers returned with
sorrowful visages, with broken noses and hats, no coats,
and all the glory departed (i.i pieces) from their gorgeous
check pants—pants so checked that whenever jou gazed
upon them you always wondered whose move it was next.
The Oberon racecourse no longer knows the hoarse voice
of the “ layer of odds,” and tire collective O'Flynn, looking
in vain for 3 to 2, and feeling a sense of loneliness starts
bashing the solitary “copper,” who unfortunately happens
to be a “ prodistin.”—“ r.';a ’Enry ’’—Wattle Flat.

Dear Bulletin, — In a late issue you have an
item re ski-running at Kiandrn, N.S.W., and you seem to
hardly credit that one thousand yards can be covered in
25 secs, on ski or snow-shoes. I hail from a country
where ski-running is the national sport, and think nothing
much of the feat mentioned. Everybody there has slid
down a steep hill on ski (the steeper, of course, the
higher the speed), and has thus felt a sensation
like falling through space, The speed of a race-horse
or of a locomotive is nothing to the pace one can get out
of ski. In Norway (no other country has got any ski-
running to talk about) they don’t give prizes for speed,
hut for the biggest jump. In the middle of the hill
there is piled up a lot of snow in the shape of a spring-
board, two or three yards high, and from this the runners
spring. The biggest officially-recorded jump, after
which the jumper has been able to keep his balance on
landing, was at the Kristiania Ski Club's meeting, some
six or seven years ago. The distance measured was
63 feet long and 22 feet high. The writer was an eye-
witness to tlie feat, and can vouch for the correctness of
the figures given. Have the Kiandra people got anything
like this as a record for jumps on ski ’ Funnel Bend.

When he last visited Australia George Loh-
mann, tlie English cricketer, was offreed a position in the
A.. 1.5. Bank if he would remain in Sydney, but he refused
the bait. It is thought unlikely lie would say “no” if
the offer were renewed. Lohmann’s father was on
the Stock Exchange, hut was “ hammered,” and
George himself was something in a stockbroker’s office
but found cricket more to his taste, ami quite as profit-
able. He lias been the main stay of Surrey during the
last season, but he does not forget that the Club was dis-
posed to treat him rather scurvily the season before, when
the rewards for services were being distributed. W. W.
Read received £lOO and Lohmann was put off with £25.
He struck at this and demanded more, and it was only
when he threatened to secede from the club that the
grant was increased to £5O. W. W.’s position is a sore
point with the Surrey professionals, for though he poses
as an amateur, or gentleman, he depends mainly on
cricket for his subsistence. He is nominally assistant
secretary of the Surrey Club, but nobody has ever seen
him at his desk, and in addition to the salary he receives
for his supposed secretarial work he gets a handsome
bonus every season for his cricket play. This amateur
business is a transparent sham.

George Alston, sen , one of the pioneer
settlers of South Australia, died last week, aged
90. He had been 53 years in the country, and at
one time campetl in a tent on the allotment where
the Plough and Harrow Hotel, Adelaide, now
stands. He was the first accountant of the Bank-
of S. A., and also secretary to the S. A. Agricultural
Society, in which latter capacity he was interested
in the importation of the first shipment of horses
into S. A.
Two clerics, Protestant and Catholic, have
been discussing in Sydney D. T. the question
whether the Church of England has retained the
Apostolic Succession. The Protestant gentleman
says it has and the priest says in effect that it
hasn’t—that it was born in the adulterous bed of
Henry VIII. The discussion winds up very
funnily. The Anglican parson has had last say :
“ If the Rev. D. F. Barry is right the Archbishop
of Canterbury is wrong.” That ought to settle
the matter.
Vernon Mid wood, the oldest Tasmanian
Civil Servant, has died of influenza, aged 72.
For 56 years he was a civil servant, and, for 50,
chief-clerk to the Tasmanian Supreme Court.
“ Old Mid wood ” was to old Hobartonians just as
familiar as Mount Wellington. His duties were
about the heaviest in the Civil Service, and will
be divided amongst his successors; His salary of
.£3OO was last year raised to .£350. Though the
most profitable officer in the service to his em-
ployers, he was, until quite recently, located in
the coldest, dreariest, and most uninviting dog-
hole to be found in the building, without fire or
aught to add comfort to his old age. Until a few
months ago, he never missed a day’s duties. He
was a model of kindliness and courtesy, and knew
more about the antecedents of the “ very best ”
families than any other man living, yet his innate
goodness of heart prevented him from saying
anything to harm a soul. He carried in his head
a whole library relating to the ancient records,
and could not be spared to retire on a pension,
so he died in harness, and much valuable informa-
tion has gone with him—as his successors will now
find.

This is unfair, because if there is one thing in which his
a l Highness really excels it is in pheasant or grouse
shooting. True, he does not tramp about with dogs, and
Jlf been knowni to sit and shoot at Sandringham ; but
fh en he never was a walker, and is far too stout now to
tramp far oyer heavy country. As for the barn-door
business, as it is called, any sportsman knojys well enough
a driven pheasant is about the most difficult bird
that flies to shoot, aiid yet the Prince rarely misses one.
He is by far the best shot in the Royal Family.
}\ n \ y ' f the Enfdishman must weigh an appalling
lot b ® f ? re h « loses h ‘ s inward yearning to’kill
something, llie spectacle of Wales, too old and
obese to walk, having birds brought to him that
he may shoot them in his arm-chair, is a miserable
show enough, but there is a lower depth still, and
he will probably get there in time. Some day he
will be too fat even to shoot, and then the
wretched rooster will be fetched along tied-up
with string, and the Heir-apparent will rise with
a heavy groan, and it will be planted on his
chair, and ho will laboriously sit upon it, and
smother it. The fine old British gentleman must
kill some animal or other that never did him any
harm, before he can feel happy, and whether it
is shot or sat upon the animal gets there just the
same.

PARIS, 1889.
W. B. Dalley.
A well-informed correspondent sends us the
following interesting gossip :
W. B. Dailey’s father kept a shop in Lower
George-street, Sydney, opposite the old Treasury,
as it was called. It was tho next house to the
“Fortune of War,” going towards Dawes Point.
His business was supplying kits for sailors, and
most of tho whaling crews, of which there were
many in those days, used to get their outfit there.
Cashing advance-notes, too, was a profitable part
of the old man’s business, and he gradually amassed
money and purchased land and houses, but he was
never what we would now call a rich man. Dailey
inherited his brains from his mother, who, though
poorly-educated, was very clever.
# # »
As a youth, W.B.D. was incorrigibly lazy, and
not by any means what would bo called sharp.
He was always the leader in any mischief. He
was forever in trouble at school, and was threatened
with expulsion from St. Mary’s Seminary, but
escaped that indignity by bolting incontinently
out of a window whilst the Rev. Dr. Gregory was
being sent for to formally expel him before his
schoolmates. The first school he was sent to was
a ladies’ seminary at the Flagstaff, kept by the
Misses L , where ho gained some knowledge
of the three R’s. Thence he was sent to St.
Mary’s, the head-master of which was the Rev.
Mr. Bourgeois, a French priest, whose severity
had, no doubt, a good deal to do with his
wild conduct, for W.B. could not bear coercion.
After bolting from St. Mary’s he studied for some
time privately. His father wanted to put him
into business, and had almost arranged to appren-
tice him to Hordern, the draper, who kept a shop
at the foot of Church Hill—Charlotte Place it
was, and may still be called. At the earnest
request of Mrs. Lett, who was very fond of the
lad and was the first to discern his ability, this
idea was abandoned, and his father having pur-
chased a barque, the Asa Packer, young Dailey
was sent to sea in her as a passenger. He had
some books on board, which he read assiduously,
and thus formed a decided taste for literature, in
which he was encouraged by the lady just named
—a woman of vex - y high mental attainments.
# * *
His father in the meantime had retired from
business with a moderate fortune, and went to
reside in one of two houses which he had built
opposite the Legislative buildings in Macquarie-
street, Sydney. Here young Dailey fell ill, and was
for long confined to bed through an affection of the
neck. During his prolonged illness he was
encouraged by his life-long friend, Mrs. Lett, to
study, and he set to work and learned Latin and
Greek. Only for a short time had he a tutor, so
it may bo truly said that he was entirely self-
educated, as he had no one but Mrs. Lett to direct
his studies. As ho became convalescent he used
to spend a portion of his time listening to the
debates in the Legislative Council —“they were
giants there in those days and having a
marvellous memory, ho was able to repeat
almost verbatim speeches he had heard delivered
by Wentworth, Lowe, Cowper, Darvall and the
other orators who were then members of the
single house of Legislature. His constant atten-
dance attracted I lie attention of !3ir Charles
Nicholson, the Speaker, and other members, and
the youngster was permitted to roam about the
Council buildings, and to take Ins seat in the
gallery without the formality of an order. Often
and often has the writer heard him repeat the
ipsissima verba of some long speech which he had
heard in the Chamber. Ho thus gradually
acquired a readiness of tongue, which increased
as he grew older, until at last he used to discard
repetition, and make speeches of his own on the
burning topics of the day, bis audience generally
being composed of his mother and father, and Mrs.
Lett. The writer was then a small boy, who
shared the worship of tho others for the young
prodigy, as he was then considered.
« * «
The advent of Deniehy with his brilliant con-
versational and oratorical powers at this lime
acted as an additional incentive to Dailey, and he
studied harder than ever. Having selected the
Law as his future profession, he devoted himself
to Blackstone, and in due course was called to the
bar in 1850. From this time forward his history
is known to everybody.
# # #
Ho was not what could be called a cultured man
in the full sense of the term. He had a fair
knowledge of Latin, but knew little of Greek, and
nothing of mathematics. He had a smattering of
French, though he could never speak it fluently,
and could read Italian. He was thus able to
learn something of the literature of France and
Italy, though he read most of the best authors in
translations. Having an extraordinary memory,
and being quick of apprehension, he readily picked
up a smattering of knowledge from men like
Stenhouse, Badham and Stephen o , with whom he
was intimate. In this way, being a good talker, he
acquired among the public the reputation of being a
profoundly read man, which he decidedly was
not. But he was a good listener, and in the pres-
ence of his superiors in knowledge he had the good
sense to remain silent and take in all that he
heard. •
* # •
As a political satirist he was only inferior to
Deniehy .and William Forster, the latter of whom
was a staunch friend and ally of his. They were
united by the bond of common hatred of Farkes,
and they took up Sydney Punch and wrote for it
without fee or reward on condition that it was
made a. party organ. He was also associated with
Allan Macpherson in the Laughing Jackass
which was started by the latter, avowedly for the
purpose of opposing Parkes. Macpherson, Dailey,
Forster, and occasionally Macleay, kept the
Jackass going until it was quietly suppressed
after a specially venomous attack on Macpherson’s
bogey Parkes, in which he was cartooned as Titus
Oates in the pillory. This was in connection with
the O’Farrell business.
* # #
I almost forget now whether Frank Hutchinson
wrote for the Laughing Jackass, but I think
not. Grosvenor Bunster did a little sometimes.
The contributors used to meet like conspirators,
at night, at Macpherson’s house in PhiJip-street.
It was a mysterious sort of place, partly under-
ground, and one had to go down a flight of steps
to the front door. Occasionally Miss Kitty
C , the actress, a friend of Macpherson’s,
used to be admitted to the conclave. The
Jackass took a prominent part in the attacks
upon Parkes, arising out of the O’Farrell case, and
the “Kiama Ghost” was worked with good
effect. It was on a hint from the police that the
paper was stopped.
Probably the finest forensic speech Dailey ever
made was in defending Dr. Benton Beatty, a
young medical man, charged with criminally
assaulting a woman patient of his at Forbes or
Grenfell, I forget which. He was tried at
Bathurst assizes, and having no friends or money,
Dailey was assigned for the defence by Mr.
Justice Cheeke. Macintosh was assigned as
solicitor in the case, and a verdict of acquittal
resulted. If ever the hand of Providence inter-
posed to save a human being’s life, it was in tins
case. The circumstances arc too long to relate
here, suffice it to say that the case for the Crown
(Butler prosecuted) was irresistible, and it was
only by tho merest acc'dent that Tom Symonds
(one of the best whips that ever handled
a team, and who drove on the Western Road
before the railway era, and afterwards kept a
public-house nearßowenfcls) came into court, and
seeing the woman recognised her, and calling
the acting Crown - Solicitor, gave him cer-
tain information which, in the interests of Jus-
tice, he communicated to the counsel for the
defence. The case was concluded at two o’clock
in the morning, when, as I have said, the accused
was acquitted. Dailey’s address to the jury was,
unfoitunately, owing to the late hour at which it
was made, never reported properly, but it was one
of the most powerful and thrilling speeches I have
ever heard in a court of justice. Butler was so im-
pressed by it that he did not exercise his right of
reply. Dailey was full of sentimentality and
chivalry, and when heinterested himself inacase the
question of fees never entered into his calculations.
He was quite as earnest in his defence of a pauper,
if there was anything in tho case which appealed
to his feelings, as if he had received 1000 guineas—
which was the amount marked on his biief for the
defence of Yivers, for horse-stealing at Armidale
Assizes.
* * 9
It was a treat to sec Dailey defending a prisoner
in a big case and old “ Paddy ” Butler prosecut-
ing, for each was unsurpassed in his own line. It
must be admitted, however, that Butler was the
superior where solid facts had to be sifted and
marshalled before a jury, and in knocking a hole
in an alibi he was unequalled, as the records of
the bushrangers’ trials will attest. •
* * #
Dailey was very partial to making speeches in
dog-Latin, which used to create considerable
amusement amongst people like Dick Hill, who
didn’t understand a word of them. It was a
strong point with him, by the way, to select an
audience which knew little of the subject he was
talking about, to be in fact a “ Triton among
minnows.” This propensity for dog-Latinity
once led him into a fool sh position. There was
at the Nortli Shore an old Catholic priest,
Dr. Powell, who was exceedingly well-edu-
cated, but very eccentric. Ho was modest
withal and did not air his extensive knowledge,
so that people did not know that the somewhat
shabby old clergyman could speak tho dead
languages colloquially with the utmost fluency.
Going down the harbour one day with a picnic
party—l think Macleay was tho host—the playful
W. 8.D., being in one of his merry moods, thought
he would “make a hare ” of the poor old padre ,
and addressed him in one of his Latin speeches.
The priest listened patiently till the oration was
concluded, and then rose and expressed his
regret that he was not so well acquainted with
the language in which Horace wrote as his young
and brilliant friend, but as he had, thanks to an
Irish hedge-school education, a slight smattering
of another classic tongue, he would take the liberty
of replying to him in it, which he proceeded to do
—to the great delight of Dr. Badham, greatest of
Greek scholars. This covered Dailey with con-
fusion. This lesson was never forgotten.
Spain has wasted five millions fortifying
the Pyrenees against France; but she will have
Gibraltar Fancy how the Divine
which watch over England exclusively, would be
outraged if Spain settled on the tip of the nose of
Land’s End!
Commissioned on a Friday, by Ward and
Lock, to write a popular biography of Mr.
Parnell, T. P. O’Connor had finished off and
handed to the publishers, on Saturday afternoon,
tho manuscript, consisting of 15,000 words. Of
course, he had the material at his fingers’ ends,
but for all that it was a marvellous achievement.
A new Eucalyptus, the Mia Mia, has just ,
been discovered, by a Mr. Nance, which promises
to revolutionise the whole medical world. Fora |
number of years Mr. Nance has been travelling i
through the wilds of Australia testing the
different leaves and shrubs, with the result that
he has at last discovered a shrub unknown to any-
one else, from which he is manufacturing this
wonderful Mia Mia Eucalypti. Judging by the
immense success and the extraordinary results
obtained by it in a short snace of time, there is a
great future in store for it.*

STILL IN THE LEAD.
The great remedy for pain, St. Jacobs Oil, is a
wonder surely. But it is the result only of tru i
medical science and research. Such a discovery
had a right to prominence. It has cured wherever
used as directed. Founded upon the intrinsic
merit of each element, which, in combination,
proved to bo the perfection of cure, its virtue haa
made it what it is.
THE PEOPLE
of the United States know its name, and name it
as a remedy which should be in every house, for
every homo knows its value, as a sterling
comforter, the truest relief,
THE WORLD OWNS IT.
Canada took it up and adopted it as a standard
cure. It crossed the ocean, on demand, and alike
in Scotland, England, and Ireland, in the extremes
of climate and temperature, it is the same
sovereign Conqueror of Pain, Distinguished men
and physicians of South and Central America,
have used and recommended it, and street-criers
have sung its praise in verso in the streets of
Mexico, A member of Parliament
IN SOUTH AFRICA
praised it in open debate, and the same was done
by a U.S. Senator. In India it won its first gold
medal at the Calcutta Exposition, where expert
chemists and physicians awarded it its due; and
by merit it has won in all similar competitions.
One of the most distinguished scholars of Germany
thought it his duty to praise it, and such has been
its meed from Prelates, Potentates, and the Press.
A distinguished traveller comments on its use in
Egypt. It was the comforter and cure of the lost,
half-frozen Arctic explorers of the Greeley expedi-
tion.
BRITISH SOLDIERS AND SAILORS.
It found its place in the knapsacks of the British
soldiers in the Soudan, and its permanent supply
to the medicine chests of her Majesty’s warships.
This would seem enough to show what it is
doing ; but one other potent fact can’t be dis-
regarded. It holds its own as the best known
remedy of tiro ago for rheumatism, neuralgia,
lumbago, sciatica, and all painful ailments. It
has taken its place in the trade of tins continent,
and has received from many of its numerous
patrons throughout these colonies, as elsewhere,
tatements that cures, made by its healing virtues,
Tom the date of cure to the date of response,
embracing a lapse of years, have remained per-
manent without recurrence.

A POPULAR MISTAKE.
Many people have an idea that the Waterbury,
because a cheap watch, is therefore cheaply made.
A visit to the company’s factory, at Waterbury,
Conn., would decidedly alter this impression. On
seeing the extent and variety of the machinery
used, and the neatness, order, and organisation
there exhibited, you begin to realise that making
a cheap watch (and a good one) is not by any
means the off-hand affair you had thought it was.
As you pass through department after department
and observe the working of wonderful automatic
machines, which make parts so fine you can
scarcely see them, and yet so accurate that they
can be taken at random and fitted together to
form a perfect watch; as you note the handling
of the parts by dainty and skilful fingers, the
delicacy of the process, and the thoroughness and
care as to every detail, you find it difficult to
imagine that they are not making hundred-dollar
watches ; and you then become convinced that the
Waterbury is cheap, not because poorly made, but
because unique and simple in construction.

The celebrated English Hozier divorce-
case, which was expected to give to the world
some queer revelations, has been squared, it is
said. It will be recollected that Mr. Ashmead
Bartlett threatened to criminally prosecute any-
one who used his name in connection with this
scandal.
Murder or Mercy?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Yes, we had got the run from our billets and
our lodgings in one day. Jim Nolan and I were
room mates and worked in the same store, and his
father had taken up a selection alongside my
father’s, so that is how we were mates.
“Look here, Jim,” I said, “let’s clear out of
town. It’s no use hunting for billets. The old
lady’ll object to my doing it because she always
reckoned on me becoming a big storekeeper, but
I’m not made for it.”
“ Well, I’m made to graft at anything that’s
got tucker hanging to it till times are better,”said
Jim.
“Well, what d’ye say to going scalping out
Womalilla way?” I proposed ; “there's whips o’
red ’tins out there, and skins fetch a good price
besides the scalp-money.”
“ I’m on,” said Jim. “ I’ll got my Winchester
from the old lady, and, I suppose, the boss’ll let us
have a few thousand rounds at trade price. What
about tents and rations? ”
“I’ll fix that,” said I, “I’ll write to the old man
to send the tents over the Chinchilla Station, and
some tucker, and we’ll get 'em as we pass. We
can get a pad horse in Mitchell, besides something
to ride.”
So wo got it all fixed up to have a long spell of
work. Next thing we were camped out on
W omalilla Creek and getting in a good stack of
skins. Jim was the hand with a knife I ever
saw. He’d make the first cut and give a few
punches with his hand, and off would come the
whole pelt, and not a scar on it but what the lead
had made.
It was a grand life. There was just enough
work to make a good living, and fresh air and sun
to give us an appetite. What on earth makes so
many men drift to town who can’t do anything
there? Oftentimes it’s a fellow’s mother, who
wants to see him a cut above a boundary-rider or
stockman, or even a selector, and sends him down
to try his luck at growing into a gentleman. 1
know it was that way with me. The old lady
wanted mo to be a “big storekeeper,” and we
boys had almost always done her wishes.
But this isn’t the story I started to tell. Jim
and I were doing well, for, not reckoning scalps,
the hides alone were worth a crown apiece. Then
one unlucky day Jim had an idea, which is a
dangerous thing in unpractised hands.
“ What d'ye say to some honey on yer bread
for supper?” he asked me.
“All right,” I replied; “send round and get
some,”
“Don't be an ass,” responded Jim; “fetch an
axe and a bucket, while I go and get my crosscut.”
Well, we started out, and came to a tree about
half-a-mile from the camp, where Jim had spotted
a bees’ nest—regular hybrids. We’d both got
mosquito-net on our hats, because the hybrids are
more savage than a mob of scrub bulls. Jim gave
the butt of the tree a thump with the axe.
“ Pretty solid down here, anyhow,” he said.
But the jar had made the swarm hum.
“ By Jove ! it’s a big swarm,” observed Jim, as
they came out unanimously. Then I ran my
eye up the trunk. It was a b’g carbeen, and went
up as straight and plumb as a rush for about forty
feet.
“ What way’ll he fall ?” I asked dubiously.
“ Better try and let the wind drop him. He’s
too plumb for my liking.”
So we ripped into him on the lee side till about
half-way across. Then we undercut it. It seemed
solid right through. Then we started on the
windward side, and we hadn’t cut more than two
inches when we were into the pipe. It had run to
one side of the tree, and all that side was a shell.
It had been struck by lightning, too, some time or
other, judging by the big limb smashed off at the
fork. We hadn’t got more than four inches into
the cut when she went “crack!” like a pistol-
shot. There was no groaning such as you hear
from a tree that has a bit of a lean.
“Run !” was all Jim said as the tree began to
dip.
Well, it seemed to drop without beginning at
all. I hadn’t waited for Jim to say “run,” but
cleared like a racehorse. Suddenly I heard a
yell, and I looked round. The crash of the tree
and that yell were nearly in one; if there was any
difference the yell was a bit ahead. I couldn't see
Jim anywhere, as the tree, instead of falling with
the wind, had got swung round by a strip of hard
deadwood which had been overgrown by green
wood. This strip was between the two cuts.
“God help the poor old chap,” I said, “for I
can’t.”
The stump of the broken limb was bearing on
his legi; they were fairly driven into the ground.
His eyes were shut, but I could see his chest still
heaving, and presently he began to groan and
writhe a little. I was trying to cut off a branch
that was nigh his face and had cut his dheek. I
was clean off my head. I didn’t know what to do.
I couldn’t cut through the top of the trunk and
saw off the main branch without cutting him.
Besides, I couldn’t have rolled the block off him
myself. I got the branch clear of his face, and I
could see that his eyes were opened. I bent over
him.
“Jim, my poor boy,” I says, “ I can’t get vou
out.”
He roused a little when he heard me speak.
“No use,” he said. “ Tommy, I’m bound for the
Never Never.”
I knew it, too. If I had got him out he would
have died all the same. Presently he gave an
awful shriek. God grant that I may never hear
the like again. The bees couldn’t get out before,
because the hole was against the ground and the
other end of the pipe in the tree was stuffed with
mud, but when I cut away that limb that was
lying on Jim’s face I had made a way for them to
get out without knowing it.
“Tommy,” he yelled, “finish me quick, for
Christ’s sake! Don’t leave me to be stung to
death ! Tommy, where’s your revolver? Quick,
shoot!”
My revolver was in its pouch on my belt. The
agony on his face and in his voice was awful.
Was it Murder or Mercy ?

The Waiting Juliet.*
(Extracted from the Memoirs of Gabriel Foote,
Highwayman.)
The house in question was what Peter the Scholar
(who corrects my proof-sheets) calls one of the
rusinurby sort —the front facing a street and the
back looking over a turfed garden, with a lime-
tree or two, a laburnum, and a lawn-tennis court
marked out, its white lines plain to see in the
starlight. At the end of the garden, a door,
painted dark-green, led into a narrow lane between
high walls, where, if two persons met, one had to
turn sideways to let the other pass. The entrance
to this lane was cut in two by a wooden post
about the height of your hip, and just beyond
this, in the high-road, George was waiting for us
with the dog-cart.
We had picked the usual time —the dinner-hour.
It had just turned dark and the church clock, two
streets away, was chiming the quarter after eight,
when Peter and I let ourselves in by the green-
door I spoke of, and felt along the wall for the
gardener’s ladder that we knew was hanging there.
A simpler job there never was. The bedroom
window on the first floor stood right open to the
night air; and inside was a faint candle-light
flickering, just as a careless maid will leave them
after her mistress has gone down to dinner. To
be sure, there was a chance of her coming back to
put them out; but we could hear her voice going
in the servants’ hall as we lifted the ladder and
rested it against the sill.
“She’s good for half-an-hour yet,” Peter whis-
pered, holding the ladder while I began to climb;
“but if I hear her voice stop, I’ll give the signal
to be cautious. ”
I went up softly, pushed my head above the
level of the sill, and looked in.
It was a roomyplace, with a great half-testerbed,
hung with curtains, standingout from thewallonmy
right. The, curtains were of chintz, a dark back-
ground with flaming red poppies sprawling over it;
and the further curtain hid the dressing-table, and
the candles on it and the jewel-case that I confi-
dently hoped to stand upon it also. A bright
Brussels carpet covered the floor, and the wall-
paper, I remember—though, for the life of me, I
cannot tell why—was a pale-gray ground, worked
up to imitate watered silk, with sprigs of gilt
honeysuckle upon it.
I looked round and listened for half a minute.
The house was still as death up here —not a sound
in the room or in the passages beyond. With a
nod to Peter to hold the ladder firm, I lifted one
leg over the sill, then the other, dropped my feet
carefully upon the thick carpet, and went quickly
round tlie bed to the dressing-table.
But at the corner, and as soon as ever I saw
round the chintz curtain, my knees gave way, and
I put out my hand toward the bed-post.
Before the dressing-table, and in front of the
big glass in which she could see my white face,
was an old lady seated.
She wore a blaze of jewels and a low gown, out
of which rose the scraggiest neck and shoulders I
have ever looked on. Her hair was thick with
black dye and fastened with a diamond star.
Between the two candles the powder showed on
her cheek-bones like flour on a miller’s coat. Chin
on hand, she was gazing steadily into the mirror
before her, and, even in my fright, I had time to
note that a glass of sherry and a plate of rice and
curry stood at her elbow, among the rouge-pots <■
and powder-puffs.
While I stood stock-still and pretty well scared
out of my wits, she rose, still staring at my image
in the glass, folded her hands modestly over her
bosom, and spoke, in a deep, tragical voice :
“ The prince !”
Then, facing sharply round, she held out her
thin arms.
“ You have come—at last ?”
There was not much to say to this except that I
had. So I confessed it. Even with the candles
behind her, I could see her eyes glowing like a
dog’s, and an uglier poor creature this world
could scarcely show.
“ Is the ladder set against the window ?”
“ Since you seem to know, ma’am,” said I, “ it
is.”
“Ah, Romeo! Your cheeks are ruddy—your
poppies are too rod.”
“ Then I’m glad my colour’s come back ; for, to
tell the truth, you did give me a turn just at first.
You were looking out for me, no doubt ”
“ My prince!” She stretched out her arms
again, and, being pretty well at my wits’ end, I
lat her embrace me. “It has been so long,” she
said . “ oh , the weary while ! And they ill-treat
me Where have you been all this tedious time?”
j was not go j ng to
answer that, you may be
sure . By this, I had recovered myself sufficiently
to guess what was near the truth—that she was a
mac j aun fc of the family below, and that the game
was j n my kands if j played with decent care. So
j met her question with another,
«Look here,” 1 said ; “ I’m running a consider-
able risk in braving these persecutors of your’s.
Hadn’t we better elope at once ? ”
“j am rea dy ”
« And the jewels? You won’t leave them to
yonr enemies I suppose ”
She turned to the dressing-table, lifted her
jewel-case, and put it into my hands.
“I am ready,” she repeated ; “ let us be quick
and stealthy as death.”
she followed me to the window and, looking
d rew back
“What horrible, black depths !”

and helped her, setting her feet on the rungs. We

door Peter could stand no more of it. _
“ Ive a fire-arm in my pocket,” whispered he,
pulling up, “ and I’m going to fire it off to relieve
my feelings, if you don’t explain here and now.
Who, in pity’s name, is she ? ”
“You mug—she’s the Original Sleeping Beauty.
I’m eloping with her, and you've got her jewels.”
“Pardon me, Jem,” he says, in his gentlemanly
way, “if I don’t quite see. Are you taking her
off to melt her or marry her? For how to get rid
of her else ”
The poor old creature had halted, too, three
paces ahead of us, and waited while we whispered,
with the moonlight, that slanted down into the
lane, whitening her bare neck and flashing on her
jewels.
“One moment,” I said, and stepped forward to
her; “you had better take off those ornaments
here, my dear, and give them to my servant to
take care of. There’s a carriage waiting for us at
the end of the lane, and when he has stowed them
under the seat we can climb in and drive off ”
“To the end of the world—to the very rim of
it, inyhero.”
She pulled the gems from her ears, hair, and
bosom, and handed them to Peter, who received
them with a bow. Next, she searched in her
pocket and drew out a tiny key. Peter unlocked
the case, and, having carefully stowed the dia-
monds inside, locked it again, handed back the
key, touched his hat, and walked off towards the
dog-cart.
“My dearest lady,” I began, as soon as we
were alone between the high walls, “if the devo-
tion of a life ”
Her bare arm crept into mine. “There is but a
little time left for us in which to bo happy. Year
after year I have marked off this almanac; day
by day I have watched the dial. I saw my sisters
married, and my sisters’ daughters ; and still I
waited. Each had a man to love her and tend
her, but none had such a man as I would have
chosen. They were none like you, my prince ! ’
“ No, I dare say not.”
“ Oh, but my heart is not so cold. Take my
hand—it is firm and strong; touch my lips—they
are burning ”
A low whistle sounded at the top of the lane.
As I took her hands 1 pushed her back, and, turn-
ing, ran for my life. I suppose that, as I ran, I
counted forty before her scream came, and then
the sound of her feet pattering after me.
She must have run like a demon ; for I was less
than ten yards ahead when Peter caught my wrist
and pulled me up on to the back seat of the dog-
cart. And before George could set the horse going,
her hand clutched at the flap on which my feet
rested. It missed its grasp, and she never got
near enough again. But for half a minute I looked
into that horrible face following us and working
with silent rage; and for half a mile at least I
heard the patter of her feet in the darkness be-
hind. Indeed, I can hear it now. Q.

“ A Study in Heredity.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I. —Two Newspaper Extracts.
April 17, 1888. —Yesterday at the Central
Criminal Court, Augustus Charles Desmond, soli-
citor’s clerk, was found guilty of forging a deed,
and was sentenced to four years’ imprisonment
with hard labour.
Sept. 29, 1891.—Yesterday, at the . . Police
, Court, Barbara Desmond, aged 22, postal assistant
at that suburb, was committed for trial on a charge
of stealing money from a letter, the property of
the Postinaster-General.
XL— Passages' From a Diary.
April 16, 1888.— How shall I pen the words?
Oh, -that I had died before I had heard that ter-
rible sentence from the Judge’s lips which sent
my father to gaol for four years. Four years !
And the shame and the disgrace of it all to be
borne for four years before we can go away to a
strange place, for mother says she will not'leave
this city till lie is freed. Up to the last, up to the
very moment of the sentence, I believed him inno-
cent, believed there had been some awful mistake
or some foul conspiracy at work. Up to the
moment? No, beyond it : till the Judge read out
the record of father’s previous conviction. His
precious conviction —O God, why hast Thou per-
mitted me to live, to know this of my father to
whom I owe duty and reverence? Thou hast laid
Thy Commandment upon me to honour him, to
obey him, and how can Ido either? But, forgive
me, O God, for this, my doubting! Thou, hast
some wise purpose in this chastisement, surely.
01), give me Thy peace and Thy forgiveness, and
teach me, O Heavenly Father, to bear the blow
patiently and still to regard him with that love
which it has been my joy to give him to now,
although —but I must not admit even to you, my
diary, what at times I fear he is.
Ap.'il 17. —Mother is calmer to-day, and forces
hersjff to think and speak more rationally of this
terrible, terrible disgrace. I resolved when I rose
this morning to. forget my own pain if I could,
and to minister to her, but I have failed miserably
—miserably. Tasked her to-night did she know
of the previous crime which the Judge said was
recorded against him. She would not answer for
a while, but I pressed her, and then she told me.
Two. years— for a! similar offence, twelve years
ago, when we lived up country. I remembered,
then ! I was a slip of a girl, just like, I suppose,
Connie Williams is now—and father was away for
a long time, and people gave up coming to see us,
and mother took me from school, and after a while
we came to the city where he joined us, I recol-
lect how I thought he had been away for a long
sea-voyage. One or other must have told me so.
How can parents lie to their children ? How dare
they have children when they are tainted so? Oh,
I’ll never marry, never ! Two sentences upon my
father, my father! Oh, the shame of it, the
wrong of it! And how dare I face my school-
class on Sunday ? They may not know, may
not understand all the ignominy if they do
—but no, children are so quick they will
know. And then there are my fellow-teachers —
how will they receive me? As though, O Lord,
my cross is not heavy enough to bear, wilt Thou
make it heavier by turning my work in Thy
service into the source of a new burden?
April 18. —After I closed my diary last night
the thought came to me that I should resign my
teachership in the Sunday-school. I fought
against it, for my work there and the preparation
for it, have been my chief, I may say, my only
pleasures, outside my home. And that is wrecked
now—and shall I rob myself of that which re-
mains? So I reasoned the best part of the night
—I scarcely slept at all. But I have made up my
mind to give it up. The necessity is very bitter,
but my disgrace will not only expose me to
humiliation, it will bring insult upon His work.
People won’t let their children be taught by the
daughter of a man who is in prison. It is very
hard to give it up, but I think it is ray duty.
After all, why should I forrow? Is it not giving
up at His command “ what most I prize?’ Ah,
there is a new truth there for me ! I have been
thinking that when “He calls us to resign ” any-
thing it must be worldly tilings and pleasures
only that is meant. Now, I perceive that He may
call upon us as a proof of our affection for Him to
abandon work in His own service. If only I
could be so sure that it is His wish that I should
give up my school-work and not merely that I am
a coward and fearful of meeting the pitying and
contemptuous looks of the other teachers and
scholars.
April 20.—Yesterday I wrote to the Superin"
tendent tendering my resignation. To-night he
called, in company with our clergyman, to request
me to withdraw it. They were both so kind and
sympathising and said such gratifying things as to
my success with my class that I would have felt
proud under any other circumstances, but how
could I feel pleased with this shame hanging over
us ? They would not hear of my giving up the
class, and said there was nothing but the sineerest
sympathy felt for mother and myself in our
grievous trouble. Mr. Lawrence made one remark
which did me so much good. I had said that the
parents of my pupils , would object to have their
children brought into contact with a girl with a
stain. Mr. Lawrence grew indignant at the
thought, and said there was another side to that
argument. If he'heard of any one man or woman
who dwelt upon my father’s wrong-doing, he
would remind him'or her that the self-sacrificing
devotion of my mother’s father would have to be
taken into account if they were going to weigh
inherited qualities. “Your grandfather’s life,
dear Miss Desmond ” —these were his very
words —“ was an education to me, and I know
to many others. Have you forgotten the story of
his carrying the sick woman for hundreds of yards
through the sleety rain to shelter, how he wrapped
his own thin coat around her, going through the
storm himself in his shirt-sleeves unprotected, and
how that exposure killed him?” This was all
news to me and I told him so. I had known that
grandpa was a poor, hard-working clergyman, but
I did not know that he had led a life “ singular,”
as Mr. Lawrence put it, “in the purity of its
philanthropy, and its heroic unselfishness,” nor
that an account of his life had been written which
—again I quote Mr. Lawrence —“ was a elassic in
the literature of wise charity.” It was very sweet
to me to hear this, but so surprising ! I had no
idea grandpa’s biography had been distinguished
by remarkable qualities. “If,” concluded the
minister, “you would leave us, Miss Desmond,
because of this present trouble, on the other hand,
do not be deaf to the call that comes from your
grandfather s career. No grandchild of his can
rightly say ‘ No ’ to a good work when it is wait-
ing to her hand.” And so, at last, I consented to
go on with my classes. May He help me to put
self out of sight and to accept fresh shame if it
comes as a new call to bear patiently the cross He
has placed upon me !
April 21.—Mr. Lawrence sent me a copy of
grandfather Pentecost’s “Life.” I spent the
afternoon and'evening reading it. Oh, how good,
how generous he was—how thoughtless of self.
Think of that man being my grandfather who
staggered through the blinding storm coatless,
who, when the small-pox patient, dying in the
miserable attic, moaned that he had no human
friend to ki.-s him “as he was going into the
dark,” stooped and pi’essed his lips to the lips that
oozed contagion and death ! 1 thank Thee, O
God, that Thou hast permitted that good man’s
blood to run in my veins as well as but, oh,
this is wrong, this is dishonouring to my father
who is still my parent, whatever he may be as a
man. . . . Tasked mother to-night why she
had never told me much about grandpa. She said
she had not dared, for father had forbidden her to
speak much about him. He—-father—had never
believed in grandpa’s sincerity—had maintained
that he must have been making money out of his
work—“ Money ! ” here cried mother. “ when
many and many a night his family went supper-
less to bed so that others might have one meal a
day!”—and he had quarrelled with grandpa
because he would not share with him what father
called “ the plunder.” How terrible must all
this have been for mother ! And then father
made her promise that she would not go in “for
the goody-goody and charitable line,” and she
lias been expecting him to interfere with mycla's-
work again and again. 1 did not know anything
of this, and I am thankful now I did not, because
the knowledge that one of my parents withheld
approval of my church-work, even from wrong
motives, would have been a trial and a distraction
to me. To think of mother’s hidden life all these
years! “To close every channel of charitable
effort against myself, child,” she said, “robbed
me of half my existence, for, like my father, I
was eager to do what I could to relieve others’
woe.” Poor mother ! Poorer father !
April 22.—How needless were my anxieties!
How ashamed I am that my carnal fears of what
the world would say crushed for a time my faith
in God’s - over-ruling Providence ! This very
trouble has been made to do me good while
humbling me. There was an additional kindness,
a new sympathy, in my fellow-teachers’ reception
of me to-day, that expressed hovy they felt for
and suffered with me. Not a word to hurt me
from anyone ! Only one reference to it, and that
from a tiny, lisping thing of five, Connie Williams’
little sister, who always comes with her into the
class. She climbed as usual into my lap, and,
stroking my face, whispered—“Poo’ Miss
De’mond ! Is ’oo papa in prison?” Connie
heard her, and pulled her away sharply before I
could interfere, for though I had been anticipating
same allusion to it, yet when it came from baby-
lips I was stung to tears. I strove to hide them,
but it was no use, and a moment after I knew,
though not seeing, that every girl in the class was
crying as well. I can never forget their sympathy.
I love them the more now that I know how fond
they are of me. It would, indeed, have cut me
to the heart, have robbed me of a great joy, to
have given up my work for them.
Sept. 18, 1891. —Father was released to-day.
Mother went and met him, and brought him
home. He is greatly changed for the worse—
more cynical, always sneering. But it is sinful to
judge him.' After I was off duty to-night, and
before I came home for tea, I went to see Connie
Williams. Poor tiling, my heart bleeds for her !
—and I. feel the more acutely for her that I can-
not help her. To think of all their money going
so quickly through the failure of that bank. No .
a penny in the house —credit with the tradesmen
stopped—and the doctor insisting that the only
hope for her is to keep her nourished by wines
and food-essences ! O God, why dost Thou permit
such things to be ! But how wicked I am to pen
a sentence like that. Of course, He permits pain
and suffering for ends which must be good
because they are His. Still, it is very, very hard
to watch dear ones in agony that might, perhaps,
be relieved if there were any money ava lable.
And to think that the manager of the bank kept
race-horses out of the depositors’ money !
Sept. 25.—Saw Connie again this evening, this
being my early evening off. Took her a few
oranges. How grateful she was ! It was piteous
to receive her thanks for so paltry a gift. The
doctor came in as 1 was leaving. 1 asked him if
there was nothing that would do her good except
wine. He said, no; wine would keep up her
strength and enable her to light the pain-. She
was dying from exhaustion, such food as was
obtainable she could not assimilate. She ought
to go into the hospital, but her mother would not
hear of it. She was too proud, she had told him,
to let her child go into an institution for paupers.
I sold my brooch to-night for ten shillings. I
must take some wine to Connie, my poor Connie,
Who has been my favourite pupil all these years.
Sept. 2tith.—Oh, how can I tell the story of
this awful day ?- and yet, I must, for i swore to
put everything down, every wrong act, every sin,
that so I might build myself up spiritually by the
exercise of self-examination. O God, what a
hypocrite I must be in Thy sight—how dare I
hope for Thy forgiveness ! But I must tell the
terrible story. This morning, when I got to the
office, I sent, first thing, a note to Connie to cheer
up, for I would bring her some wine - I intended
buying it, after I came off duty this afternoon,
and taking it round. At noon, however, when I
went home for luncheon, father asked me what
money I had—l told him I had only a shilling in
hand besides the ten shillings I received for the
brooch, and said for what purpose I designed that
sum. He insisted upon my giving him the money;
he had not given me jewellery to sell to keep
other people’s brats in luxury, he said. “I was
following in my dear grand-father Pentecost’s
footsteps, was I? ” No ; not if he knew it! He
didn’t choose that a daughter of his should be—
A nerve o’er which did creep,
The else unfelt oppressions of mankind.
That was the wfeakest sentiment Shelley ever gave
utterance to, he thought. But, of course, I didn’t
know Shelley—Shelley was too wicked a poet for
a young saint like me to appreciate, though he
was worth five thousand mawkish, Bible-inspired
Tennysons. And he went on like this all lunch-
hour. O God, forgive him for his words and for
taking the money !
Sept. 27., 6.30 a.in.—l could not finish my story
of yesterday—it was too shameful to go down, all
of it at once ! But I will force myself to do it!
Perhaps I would not have had so horrible a night
had I dared to face the terrible truth. I have
never slept. Here it is—here is the truth ! O
Diary—l am a thief ! There, the word is down.
Why does it not burn a hole in the page, like the
fact is burning into my conscience? This is how
it all occurred. When I returned to the office
after lunch yesterday I was distressed to under-
stand how I could keep my word to Connie. I
knew how she would look forward not only to my
visit, but to the receipt of the wine—how the mere

Sept. 27, 9 p.m.—l have been arrested ! Bor
the last time I write in my diary. I was seen to
take the note by the telegraph-operator, who
informed the P.M., who followed me to the
grocer’s, and saw me cash the note. . . • Ihey
came for me at lunch-time to-day when I was at
home. Mother fainted at the dreadful intelli-
gence, but father —oh, God, why hast Thou cursed
me with such a father ?—he laughed and, taking a
cigar —bought with my money ! —out of his mouth,

lll.— Newspaper Extract.
October 31, 1891.—At the General Sessions
yesterday, the girl Desmond, late postal assistant
at Post-office, accused of stealing money
from a letter, pleaded guilty to the charge. The
Crown-Prosecutor handed to the presiding Judge
a Diary kept by the prisoner, which had been
seized by the police, and which, it is understood,
contained entries which were virtually a confession
of the crime. Had it been necessary to call
evidence, the book would have been adduced in
support of the accusation. His Honor, without
comment, ordered the prisoner to be released
under the provisions of the “First Offenders
Probation Act,” and the statutory recognisances
were entered into by the- young woman’s father
and the Rev. J. Laurence.
PRICE WARUNG.
“The Bulletin ” Christinas Number.
The Christmas Number of The Bulletin will be
issued on 17th December.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will embody
original literary contributions from twenty of
the foremost Australian writers, and illustra-
tions from all the representative Australian
artists in black-and-white.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will not con-
sist of cheap imported stories shared with a
hundred other papers throughout the world.
The Bulletin Christmas Number will not be
made up of one prize-story and a prize-
picture and a lot of other matter and
pictures procured gratis from confiding “com-
petitors.”
The Christmas Bulletin, though double the size
of the ordinary weekly issue, will be published
at the usual price, Sixpence.
Agents will please send in their orders before
sth December.
People living where there is no news agent, can
have the Xmas issue mailed by remitting
Sevenpence in stamps.
HE LIGHTS HIS PIPE ONCE MORE.
Some fifty years ago the English ship “Argos’
was wrecked on a low-lying key or sand island of
the Bahama group. Only one man, a sailor, was
thrown safely on the beach by the waves. In his
pocket was a tin tobacco-box, a pipe, and a flint
and steel. Wringing the water from his clothes,
he seated himself, lighted his pipe, and with true
British phlegm proceeded to consider his situa-
tion. The moral of which is that when an English-
man, having the means, refuses to smoke, he is in
very bad form.
Eor example, here is a man who says, “ I always
enjoyed my pipe, but now I couldn’t take a whiff.”
To be sure, there was a reason, and he puts it in
this way. “Up to Michaelmas, 1887,” he says,
“I was a strong, healthy man. About this time
I began to feel bad at my stomach and had no
relish for food. I had a bad taste in the mouth,
and after eating I would retch and vomit until
the water oozed out of my eyes; and so bad was
this that my wife had to hold my head. My eyes
turned yellow, and I felt low, weak, and nervous.
Sometimes I would break out into a sweat and
then go cold and chilly.
“ I couldn’t touch solid food, and for months I
belched up sour water; and what I did eat lay
heavy upon, and seemed to be dead and lifeless in
my body. Feeling low and depressed, I had no
enjoyment in company.
“ Before this 1 teas ahvays cheerful and enjoyed
my pipe, hut now I couldn't take a whiff.
“I had a craving, gnawing pain at the stomach,
which nothing eased for long together. I had
bran poultices applied, and took different kinds of
medicine, but nothing went to the spot, nothing
eased me. After a time I had to give up my work,
for I got so weak and nervous I couldn't hit my
work iron, and my tools and things flew out of my
hand.
“ For over four months I did not have a single
good night’s rest. I would turn and turn in bed
all night long, and my wife and I often sat up the
best part of the night rather than go to bed. I
went so thin that my clothes fairly hung upon me.
Friends who cam.e to see me told one another I
could not get better, and even my wife said I
would do no more work in this world.
“ For more than twelve months I had a clever
doctor attending me, but his medicine did me no
good ; then I went to see a physician at Sudbury,
with the same result. The doctors sounded my
lungs, but found nothing wrong, and said all my
ailments were brought on by bad digestion,
“ I now got weaker and weaker, and had given
up all hopes of recovery, when in the spring of
1889 a lady from London who was staying at the
vicarage at Otten Belchamp heard of my condi-
tion. She called at Mr. Goody’s, the medicine
dealer, and told him to supply me with some
Mother Siegel’s Curative Syrup and she would
pay for it. Very reluctantly I took the medi-
cine, for I had tried so many tilings without
obtaining any benefit. However, my wife pressed
me so hard that at last I began to take the Syrup.
After a few doses I said to my wife, 1 1 think 1
feel better for this medicine,' and from that time I
commenced to improve. By'the time I had taken
three bottles I was back at my work, as strong
and well as ever—much to the surprise of every-
body.
“ People all about said I would never get well,
but I did, thank the Lord.
“Now I tell everyone that Mother Seigel's
Syrup saved my life. I can now eat anything,
and feel so light-hearted I could jump over a five-
barred gate. My neighbours all say I look ten
years younger. ”
Elias Bland, shoemaker,
Belchamp St. Paul, Clare, Suffolk.
The doctors who attributed Mr. Bland’s illness
to indigestion and dyspepsia were quite right.
All they Jacked was the proper remedy. This
appeared in Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and now our
good friend enjoys his pipe as he. did in other
years. If he is ever handled in that way again wo
may wager he won’t forget what to do.

The latest game at Ostend is managed
thus : Each player selects a perfectly fresh oyster,
places it on his plate, and watches it from a
distance. Stakes are put up all round, and the
pool goes to the man whose oyster yawns first. A
game sometimes lasts three hours—
The late Pope Hennessy was a versatile
man, and in his time played many parts. He
was once concerned in a sensational incident
which will bear relating. During the Third
Empire a Mr. Dayton was the TJ.S. Minister to
France. This gentleman was epris of a certain
very charming and clever person, a Mrs. St. John
Ecoles, and was in the habit of visiting her fre-
quently at Meurice’s, where she lived in great
style. One evening, after a dinner or ball at the
Tuileries, Mr. Dayton called upon his inamorata,
and, fatigued, threw himself upon a sofa. After
sometime, the lady, wondering why he had slept
so soundly, tried to awaken him, when, to her
horror, she discovered that he was dead. Here
was a dramatic situation ! One can imagine her
feelings at thus finding herself alone face to face
with the corpse of her lover, who, only a few
minutes before, had appeared to be in robust
health. What was to be done? To call the
hotel people would be to raise a scandal, which
was the last thing she desired. Never surely
—off the stage—was a woman in such a posi-
tion. She was fairly racked with terror, when
she suddenly bethought herself of a young gentle-
man whom she had met at dinner a few nights
previously, and who had amused her very much
by his brilliant conversation. He was staying at
Meurice’s, so she determined to confide in him
and ask his assistance, Fortunately for her, he
was in his room, and, hearing- her story, he at
once offered to do what he could to help her out
of her difficulty. After cogitating for a while he
went out, and in a short time returned with some
men, who carried the body away. So quietly was
it done that no one in the hotel save the night-
porter, whom a few francs induced to hold Ins
tongue, had the least idea what was going on.
The young gentleman was Pope Hennessy, then
starting to carve his own fortune. It appears that
he went straight to the nearest commissaire of
Police, and representing himself as a medical
man, told him that the American Minister had
been suddenly seized with a fit at Meurice’s,
and it was absolutely necessary that he
should be at once taken home, but that
at that hour of the night it was impossible
to find people to carry him ; he, therefore, craved
the assistance of Monsieur la Commissaire. That
functionary was all politeness, and was, of course,
desirous to serve so distinguished a member of
the diplomatic corps, so he sent some of his men,
who transported the body to the. Minister’s house,
suspecting nothing, and little thinking that they
were carrying a cadavrc. And so, thanks to
Hennessy’s ready wit, a very grave scandal was
avoided. The next day, of course, the papers had
the startling news that the U.S. representative
had died suddenly in his house after returning
from the palace, and so the occurrence passed out
of memory. Whether Pope Hennessy confided in
the Commissaire, and told him the truth, only
those in the secret knew. It is certain, however,
that Hennessy earned the undying gratitude
of a somewhat remarkable and very pretty woman,
who had in those days considerable influence in
more than one quarter.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
T B e Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal ,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap) nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State, Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
Ihe direct_ election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System-one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
Ihe Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility.’)
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
mu has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
papier-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
.The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after-the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
pmblication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or lessjservile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the buslnhan of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest amt most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

A well-known Sydney lawyer tells the
story that he only advised a man to plead guilty
once during his career. It was the plainest case
of horse-stealing on record, and Mr. Lawyer got a
promise from the Crown that if the prisoner
pleaded guilty a heavy sentence would not be
pressed for. And—whisper it low -a promise was
wrung even from the Judge to that effect. A
couple of hours before the case came on the lawyer
saw his client, advised him to plead guilty, and
told him what he had done, “ Mr. So-and-So,”
said the man, “ I engaged you to defend me, and
I intend to plead not guilty.” An ingenious
defence was worked up on the spur of the moment,
and the jury brought in “Not Guilty” without
leaving the box.
Thus an Indian writer :
To the young married woman nothing is more delight’
ful than making fresh incursions into the realms of
bachelordom. Having already successfully proved her
powers of captivation, she desires again to test her
ability to enthral, and to discover whether she can sub-
jugate the susceptible heart of man. Therefore, she goes
forth mashing and to mash ; hut instinct tells her that
conquest will be made more easy if she poses as a single
maiden. To this end she removes the golden symbol of
the eternity of the marriage covenant, and, dangling her
glove in her hand, lays herself out for sport. Neither has
she long to wait before the fly is attracted to the honey-
pot. Buzzing around her in a series of preliminary
oglings, the victim is soon at her feet, or rather at her
side, and quickly enough a conversation is commenced.
This rapidly ripens into a flirtation, more or less pro-
nounced according to the amorousproclivities of the male
victim, who fancies himself immensely when he finds that
he is quite a hero of the veni, vidi, vici description.
Castles in the air of the most magnificent proportion are
built by him with the greatest celerity, but they all come
down with the speed of a jerry-villa, when a tall,
bronzed fellow walks up and changes the duet into a trio.
The new arrival is instantly introduced as “ My husband,”
while by some subtle species of legerdemain a wedding-
ring appears upon the lady’s linger. Is she abashed,
disconcerted, or provoked by the sudden appearance of

ADDRESS TO A LAME ASS.
(By a Bulletincer.)
Long-winded scrawler “on the Age,”
Traducer slow and solemn,
Who can’t express your spite and rage
In less than, say, a column—
Squat on the old Repentance stool
And listen, dunderheaded fool!
What chance have you with weakaouse
The public mind to bias
You yarn and yarn, but what’s the use,
Poor slave of “ Ananias ? ”
You’ll ne’er possess the pointed pen
That tickles up one’s fellow-men.
’Tis hard to have a bitter mind
Without a bit-ter style ;
To envy wit and still to find
You can’t provoke a smile ;
Ours, doubtless, is the very bin
You’d like to shoot your rubbish in.
Perhaps you tried it in the past
And were, sans thanks, “declined
You gnashed your teeth, but now at last
An open bin you find ;
Great Syme does not refuse your screed —
He thinks it very good indeed.
Stick strictly to the daily rags—
No other hope remains
For you, whose dreary “ copy ” drags,
And head is void of brains ;
Though, maybe, you may have the luck
To dabble in the Leader muck.
Write on. O person dull and dense,
And teach Australian youth
To ne’er expect much simple sense,
Or honesty, or truth,
In heavy journals, which they buy
Because they want the “latest”—lie.
Meanwhile The Bulletin will sow
The seed with tireless hand,
And where the Age can never go,
In many a distant land,
Will people read this little rhyme
That is so rough on you and Syme.
